From scratchcomputing at gmail.com  Sun Apr  1 02:53:41 2007
From: scratchcomputing at gmail.com (Eric Wilhelm)
Date: Sun, 1 Apr 2007 02:53:41 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] usb->usb disk mirroring
In-Reply-To: <20070401040244.3580.qmail@web52410.mail.re2.yahoo.com>
References: <20070401040244.3580.qmail@web52410.mail.re2.yahoo.com>
Message-ID: <200704010253.41465.ewilhelm@cpan.org>

# from john morgali
# on Saturday 31 March 2007 09:02 pm:

>I want to clone one usb attached hard
>drive to another usb attached hard drive.

The best way to copy from one usb device to another is to get a usb 
device crossover cable with an inductive serial tap.  Attach one of the 
devices to reversed-polarity AC current to initiate the peer-to-peer 
mode.  Then attach the receiving end of the IST to your serial port and 
connect to it with your favorite terminal emulator.  Follow the 
on-screen instructions.

Of course, you might not be able to get all of the needed hardware until 
April 2nd.  By then the technology will have moved on and this 
procedure won't work anymore.  Should you find yourself in such a 
situation, there's always 'man dd', though you'll only get half of the 
transfer rate that way.

--Eric
-- 
"Unthinking respect for authority is the greatest enemy of truth."
--Albert Einstein

to flash alternate firmware (like dd-wrt or openwrt).  The Meraki runs
linux under the covers too, and is supported these days by OpenWrt's
development branch, I think.

From plug_0 at robinson-west.com  Mon Apr  2 17:01:25 2007
From: plug_0 at robinson-west.com (plug_0 at robinson-west.com)
Date: Mon, 02 Apr 2007 17:01:25 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Helo policy...
Message-ID: <20070402170125.lzxwc4v808w40g08@web.robinson-west.com>

RFC 821                                                      August 1982
                                            Simple Mail Transfer Protocol

    3.5.  OPENING AND CLOSING

       At the time the transmission channel is opened there is an
       exchange to ensure that the hosts are communicating with the hosts
       they think they are.

       The following two commands are used in transmission channel
       opening and closing:

          HELO <SP> <domain> <CRLF>

          QUIT <CRLF>

       In the HELO command the host sending the command identifies
       itself; the command may be interpreted as saying "Hello, I am
       <domain>".

Okay, but a lot of people seem to think that helo checking is futile and
more trouble than it's worth.  I run a postfix relay on my firewall  
perimeter where I wonder what the best practice is in this day and  
age.  I asked Opus
and got a completely worthless answer back of, "you can do whatever you want."
By domain in this excerpt, there is not enough context to know what is being
referred to specifically.  Is it a DNS domain name or could it be some other
kind?  How about rejecting a helo of localhost, friend, of the domain name
of my own server?

If one drops to just using spamassassin to tag spam, the problem with
this approach is that spam isn't left on the spammer's server.  Even
if you have procmail shove suspected spam into a spam folder, it is
still on your system.  The beauty of accept and classify is that you
still receive if the tests are wrong, but I have yet to see
spamassassin be wrong.

What is EHLO compared to HELO?

Michael C. Robinson

----------------------------------------------------------------
This message was sent using IMP, the Internet Messaging Program.

In-Reply-To: <20070329164633.fwjekail28gk8so4@xerxes.robinson-west.com>
References: <20070329164633.fwjekail28gk8so4@xerxes.robinson-west.com>

On Thu, 29 Mar 2007 16:46:33 -0700
plug_0 at robinson-west.com dijo:

> Someone has been trying to run a Realtek gigabit
> ethernet card and running into trouble with it
> locking up.
> 
> In general, why do some hardware devices lock the Linux
> kernel up?
> 
> I've heard IRQ conflicts and the like, but haven't
> IRQ's been kinda hokey since the dawn of PCI?  Doesn't
> the IRQ system go back to the 80's when PC's were first
> coming out, back when 640k of memory was considered
> ample?  Anyone need 2 gigabytes of ram to run their Vista
> box ;-)  It's been a long time since 15 interrupts could
> accomodate every hardware device on a PC easily.  Indeed,
> especially with the way most sound cards waste resources
> and with the added resource load of USB, IRQ sharing is
> very common these days.
> 
> I expect with 64 bit platforms that the old: IRQ, memory
> mapping, and port mapping standards are going to be
> replaced.  One option I can think of is making the
> expansion bus a serial line running at 8 Gbps.
> 
> I'm curious why Linux can't avoid locking up in some cases
> if for example the network card is poorly supported or
> the sound card is problematic?  I've had problems myself
> with a RealTek 8180 pci wireless card locking up my Linux
> system.  I am not very impressed with Realtek in general.

I don't have the knowledge of how stuff works to offer much
conversation here, but I do have a Realtek gigabit. It's an RTL-8169SC
on an Abit NF-M2S motherboard. Luckily I can disable it in the BIOS and
substitute a 10/100 card from Free Geek. If I enable it in the BIOS the
boot process locks up with a kernel panic over the IRQ. There may be
some boot options I could use that would help the situation, but I
haven't had time to do any experimenting. Maybe the cause of the
problem is because the RTL-8169 is on the motherboard instead of being
in a PCI slot. But hell, I don't even know what an IRQ is. Probably
some obscure alternate acronym for PITA.

I always thought Realtek was well supported on Linux. So when I saw
that the gigabit on the NF-M2S was Realtek I just assumed I would be
OK. My laptop has Realtek ethernet too, and it always works perfectly
regardless of what distro I try. There's a lot of chatter on the net
about the RTL-8169 and Linux. Some people have gotten it working, but
their fixes didn't work for me. I note also that it doesn't work any
better with 32-bit distros.

Should Linux be made to ignore problems like this and boot anyway? It
would be some help, but it doesn't solve the real problem.

> Okay, but a lot of people seem to think that helo checking is futile and
> more trouble than it's worth.  I run a postfix relay on my firewall
> perimeter where I wonder what the best practice is in this day and
> age.  I asked Opus
> and got a completely worthless answer back of, "you can do whatever you want."
> By domain in this excerpt, there is not enough context to know what is being
> referred to specifically.  Is it a DNS domain name or could it be some other
> kind?  How about rejecting a helo of localhost, friend, of the domain name
> of my own server?
>
> If one drops to just using spamassassin to tag spam, the problem with
> this approach is that spam isn't left on the spammer's server.  Even
> if you have procmail shove suspected spam into a spam folder, it is
> still on your system.  The beauty of accept and classify is that you
> still receive if the tests are wrong, but I have yet to see
> spamassassin be wrong.

This is one of the better guides I have found to this [1].  There a
quite a few things that get checked for depending on the server.  The
nice thing about rejecting mail immediately instead of down the line
with spamassassin is that the sender always gets feedback instead of
just a "why didn't you respond to my mail you jerk on the phone a week
later".  I find myself having to tweak, and retweak  the mail, and DNS
servers frequently to satisfy the receiving servers requirements.

From heinlein at madboa.com  Wed Apr  4 10:39:49 2007
From: heinlein at madboa.com (Paul Heinlein)
Date: Wed, 4 Apr 2007 10:39:49 -0700 (PDT)
Subject: [PLUG] Helo policy...
In-Reply-To: <ec0b5e080704041004w48e1fcb5m92f70ddc8ab6bb04@mail.gmail.com>
References: <20070402170125.lzxwc4v808w40g08@web.robinson-west.com>
	<ec0b5e080704041004w48e1fcb5m92f70ddc8ab6bb04@mail.gmail.com>
Message-ID: <alpine.OSX.0.83.0704041023020.18426@dyn-50-74.galois.com>

On Wed, 4 Apr 2007, Daniel Johnson wrote:

> The nice thing about rejecting mail immediately instead of down the 
> line with spamassassin is that the sender always gets feedback 
> instead of just a "why didn't you respond to my mail you jerk on the 
> phone a week later".  I find myself having to tweak, and retweak the 
> mail, and DNS servers frequently to satisfy the receiving servers 
> requirements.

Using sendmail -- which I realize isn't as fashionable these days as 
Postfix et al. -- SpamAssassin can be run as a milter. Its tests are 
run during the SMTP transaction and spam can be rejected with an SMTP 
550 message. The sending MTA, not the sending user, is notified of the 
rejection.

Assuming the MTA is legit, and not merely a zombie bot, the right 
person will be told that the message wasn't delivered because it looks 
and smells like spam.

If the MTA is actually a bot, it might decide to try again, or it 
might just continue on its merry (?) way like a telemarketer after a 
hang-up.

-- 
Paul Heinlein <> heinlein at madboa.com <> http://www.madboa.com/


From eehouse at eehouse.org  Wed Apr  4 11:36:06 2007
From: eehouse at eehouse.org (eehouse.org)
Date: Wed, 4 Apr 2007 11:36:06 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Linux on HP Slimline s7700n; Fry's returns
Message-ID: <20070404183606.GD2770@amethyst.spiritone.com>

In what I hoped would be the final step in my search for a small,
quiet, 64-bit, dual-core, VT-enabled (whew) machine, I bought an
s7700n at Fry's on Sunday.  I knew I might be returning it since it's
advertised as having an nvidea video subsystem.  We have a bunch of
Dells at work with nvidea chips, some of which work beautifully with
the FOSS drivers and some of which suck.

The s7700n sucked, even with the nvidia-supplied binary drivers in
Ubuntu.  It turned out that there's a bunch of other nvidea stuff on
there as well: sound, memory controller, ethernet, etc.  (Shoulda
saved the output of lspci.  Duh!)  Ethernet worked fine, as did memory
(obviously).  Sound did not work at all.  A few years back I'd have
spent hours or days updating drivers and tweaking kernel compile
options attempting to make the thing work, but I decided to return it
and to give my money to manufacturers that use Linux-friendly parts.
I'll get a Dell with Intel chips, more-n-likely.

A couple of days ago googling for 'linux HP slimline s7700n' didn't
turn anything up.  I hope this post helps with that a bit.

Anyway, returning the thing to Fry's was painless, but made me think
they haven't quite figured out Vista yet.  The machine came without
any media, but you're allowed to make a single set of "recovery" CDs
or DVDs.  One set, and that will work only on your machine.  After
you've made the set, even if you reinstall from that set, you can't
make another.  Since I was reformatting the drive to install Linux I
made the set, and used it to restore the drive before returning it.
But Fry's, after verifying that the machine was fine and reformatting
from the recovery partition, reboxed it with a discount price and
will, I assume, sell it to somebody who will eventually try to make
recovery disks.  I wonder how long it'll take folks to figure out
what's wrong.

(HP will sell you a set of disks for about $15 shipped.  Let's hope
Fry's discount on the box is more than that. :-)

--Eric

On Wed, Apr 04, 2007 at 10:17:06AM -0700, Scott Howard wrote:
> Can someone recommend a linux software program to "ghost" a drive.  The
> goal is to put the hard drive contents on a bootable cd or dvd once we

If you mean you'd like to make a backup of your FAX server's hard drive,
Mondo Rescue can take care of that, making a bootable CD or DVD that can
restore the HDD from bare-metal.

If you're using a modern distro, you should be able to use
yum/apt-get/urpmi/whatever to install "mondo" or "mondorescue".



From mikeraz at patch.com  Wed Apr  4 13:03:17 2007

Subject: [PLUG] remote X diagnostics
Message-ID: <63280.170.135.112.12.1175716997.squirrel@mail.patch.com>

I run an X server, Exceed, on my desktop PC at work.  This is needed for some
programs that use an X based interface.  Occasionally I'll pop up the odd gvim
session for editing too.

So I know the X server setup is correct and will accept connections from numerous
hosts in our network.

Except for one.  I launch the X based program from it, nhiConsole, and the program
runs and reports to the terminal window that soon the X session will start.  Then
it doesn't.  PS shows the program running, I'm just not having the session pop up
on my PC.  I double, triple check the DISPLAY environment variable on the client
computer and it's fine.

Meanwhile the guy in the next cube is using the same software stack and getting a
session on his PC with no problem.  I'm also going to a different server and
running the same command and everything works fine.

What's the next thing to do in trouble shooting this problem?

My exceed log doesn't show anything.

   Michael Rasmussen, Portland, Ore, USA
  Be Appropriate && Follow Your Curiosity

From creswick at gmail.com  Wed Apr  4 13:18:15 2007
From: creswick at gmail.com (Rogan Creswick)
Date: Wed, 4 Apr 2007 13:18:15 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] remote X diagnostics
In-Reply-To: <63280.170.135.112.12.1175716997.squirrel@mail.patch.com>
References: <63280.170.135.112.12.1175716997.squirrel@mail.patch.com>
Message-ID: <c834ec80704041318n788e9ce6r6673f32f52ba925@mail.gmail.com>

On 4/4/07, Michael Rasmussen <mikeraz at patch.com> wrote:
> Except for one.  I launch the X based program from it, nhiConsole, and the program
> runs and reports to the terminal window that soon the X session will start.  Then
> it doesn't.  PS shows the program running, I'm just not having the session pop up
> on my PC.  I double, triple check the DISPLAY environment variable on the client
> computer and it's fine.

Can you run xclock correctly on that machine? How are you connecting
to that host? Is your coworker using nhiConsole off the same server,
and using the same remote connection method? (iirc, ssh servers have
to be configured to do X redirection, if that's what you're doing --
it could be setup incorrectly on that host, or you may have a personal
setting in your home dir on that machine that disables X
redirection--I'm not sure that last thing is possible, but it might
be.)

If all that checks out, nhiConsole may be a script that tries to do
something smart and set a dynamic DISPLAY variable, or --DISPLAY param
based on your environment.  If this is buggy it could be returning the
wrong display.

This is almost certainly *not* the problem, but the app _is_ called
nhiConsole -- is there a nhiGui that may be more reliable?

--Rogan

> session on his PC with no problem.  I'm also going to a different server and

                                     with
                  Linux disk imaging and bare-metal restore

                                      by

               Jerome Boutaud              Stephan Lawton
               Solutions Engineer          Senior Director of
               Acronis Inc.                    Strategic Marketing
                                           Acronis Inc.

      Last month Brian Martin from Martin Consulting discussed his
      personal experience with disaster recovery in a real world
      situation.  This month Jerome Boutaud and Stephan Lawton
      will be discussing and demonstrating the nuts and bolts
      of disaster recovery with different Linux distributions.
      They will also discuss the differences between image-based
      backup and file-based backup, why the distinction is so
      important in the recovery phase of a disaster, and the
      advantages and disadvantages of each.

From carla at bratgrrl.com  Wed Apr  4 17:49:23 2007
From: carla at bratgrrl.com (Carla Schroder)
Date: Wed, 4 Apr 2007 17:49:23 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] DMZ and iptables
Message-ID: <200704041749.23121.carla@bratgrrl.com>

I've gone all wacko and insecure over my iptables rules, which I do on a 
regular basis. Here's the deal: a 3-port router with:

WAN - 22.33.44.55
LAN - 192.168.1.0/24
DMZ - 192.168.2.0/24

I want tight controls on traffic between the LAN and DMZ. So I need two 
FORWARD rules, then INPUT rules for specific services, correct? Like this 
example for a Web server in the DMZ. What I want to do is allow LAN clients to 
access the HTTP server in the DMZ, and nothing else:

$ipt -A FORWARD -i $LAN_IFACE -o $DMZ_IFACE -m state --state 
NEW,ESTABLISHED,RELATED -j ACCEPT

$ipt -A FORWARD -i $DMZ_IFACE -o $LAN_IFACE -m state --state 
ESTABLISHED,RELATED -j ACCEPT

$ipt -A INPUT -p tcp -i $DMZ_IFACE -s 192.168.1.0/24 --dport 80 -j ACCEPT

Or am I making a big hash of things?

-- 
~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~
Carla Schroder
Linux geek and random computer tamer
check out my Linux Cookbook! 
http://www.oreilly.com/catalog/linuxckbk/
best book for sysadmins and power users
~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~


From mikeraz at patch.com  Wed Apr  4 18:39:01 2007
From: mikeraz at patch.com (Michael Rasmussen)
Date: Wed, 4 Apr 2007 18:39:01 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] DMZ and iptables
In-Reply-To: <200704041749.23121.carla@bratgrrl.com>
References: <200704041749.23121.carla@bratgrrl.com>
Message-ID: <20070405013901.GB24843@patch.com>

Carla Schroder wrote:
> WAN - 22.33.44.55
> LAN - 192.168.1.0/24
> DMZ - 192.168.2.0/24
> 
> I want tight controls on traffic between the LAN and DMZ. So I need two 
> FORWARD rules, then INPUT rules for specific services, correct? Like this 
> example for a Web server in the DMZ. What I want to do is allow LAN clients to 
> access the HTTP server in the DMZ, and nothing else:
> 
> $ipt -A FORWARD -i $LAN_IFACE -o $DMZ_IFACE -m state --state 
> NEW,ESTABLISHED,RELATED -j ACCEPT

This allows any type of traffic. You want to allow ssh, telnet, ftp, ... to
the Web server?

> $ipt -A FORWARD -i $DMZ_IFACE -o $LAN_IFACE -m state --state 
> ESTABLISHED,RELATED -j ACCEPT

Yes, that fits your verbal description.

> $ipt -A INPUT -p tcp -i $DMZ_IFACE -s 192.168.1.0/24 --dport 80 -j ACCEPT

So you can get at the web server from the firewall?

Surplus Gizmos in North Plains may have one.  Mike has a few laptop
adapters for random machines, and they are sorted and organized.

Free Geek probably has one, but it will be close to impossible to find
one in the chaos.

Ebay is where I get my spare adapters.  I always have spares scattered
around, they take a fraction of a watt and it is easier to leave them
plugged in then climb furniture to get at them.  It is also a good place
to research which adapters work, since the sellers usually list the
machines that a particular adapter works with.

> quiet, 64-bit, dual-core, VT-enabled (whew) machine, I bought an
> s7700n at Fry's on Sunday.  I knew I might be returning it since it's

> the FOSS drivers and some of which suck.
>

> Ubuntu.  It turned out that there's a bunch of other nvidea stuff on
> there as well: sound, memory controller, ethernet, etc.  (Shoulda

> (obviously).  Sound did not work at all.  A few years back I'd have
> spent hours or days updating drivers and tweaking kernel compile
> options attempting to make the thing work, but I decided to return it
> and to give my money to manufacturers that use Linux-friendly parts.
> I'll get a Dell with Intel chips, more-n-likely.
>
> A couple of days ago googling for 'linux HP slimline s7700n' didn't
> turn anything up.  I hope this post helps with that a bit.
>
> Anyway, returning the thing to Fry's was painless, but made me think
> they haven't quite figured out Vista yet.  The machine came without
> any media, but you're allowed to make a single set of "recovery" CDs
> or DVDs.  One set, and that will work only on your machine.  After
> you've made the set, even if you reinstall from that set, you can't
> make another.  Since I was reformatting the drive to install Linux I
> made the set, and used it to restore the drive before returning it.
> But Fry's, after verifying that the machine was fine and reformatting
> from the recovery partition, reboxed it with a discount price and
> will, I assume, sell it to somebody who will eventually try to make
> recovery disks.  I wonder how long it'll take folks to figure out
> what's wrong.
>
> (HP will sell you a set of disks for about $15 shipped.  Let's hope
> Fry's discount on the box is more than that. :-)
>
> --Eric

On this note, I bought a HP DV6253 (Costco special in February) and ran
into similar problems installing OpenSuSE (10.2 and 10.3 Alpha), Debian
(both Sarge and Etch) and Ubuntu (6.10 and 7.04 Beta).

My problems included: No Wireless, Reading SD cards from the SD slot was
touchy depending on the distro, Screen Resolution (it's a 15.4
Widescreen), and a few others.  The wireless may be traced to the fact
that I have 2Gb of RAM and I saw a post about the wireless chipset (I'm at
work so I don't have the info in front of me) with more than 1Gb RAM (and
possibly 64 bit AMDs) on the kernel list.

It looks like, in general, HP is using drivers and chipsets that aren't
fully supported.  (We had similar problems with a brand new HP at work,
which was also a 64-bit AMD, that I spent some time troubleshooting.)

Oh, and don't go to HP for support!  I've spent almost 4 hours dealing
with HP to fix a "simple" problem with a bad haddrive (on another warranty
system).  All I wanted was a replacement after I gave them the error
message.  Instead the marked the problem as closed/resolved.  <sigh> 
Finally got support through email, since the VOIP to India sucked (not to
mention both the language barrier and my hearing problem) and no one
responded through their "vaunted" web support.</rant>

Anyway, I'm off-topic (and probably modded down as a troll), but it looks
like the efforts by HP (and some other unnamed vendors) to roll out new
technology / latest versions is impacting the FOSS community.

In the meantime, I'm going to patiently wait for fixes to some of the FOSS
software (kernel and a few other things) before I rebuild my new laptop
again.

Tim

I am disappointed that HP and Intel want to hide stuff and lack the shutzpah
to release a direct open source competitor to Microsoft's .NET framework.

This mono project sounds interesting, except for the closed libraries part.

Microsoft's the GPL is bad rhetoric because, "you can't have trade secrets,"
seems to miss a major strength of the license.  Secrecy means that the
potential customer can't have the software effectively evaluated by
a third party.  Well written source code is probably easier to study than
machine code any day.

What does .NET have over the alternatives anyways?  Is it true that 1 job
offer in Portland will ask for Linux and another 9 or so will request .NET?
It came up on a discussion list from a student defending the Microsoft
centricity of a course I'm taking online.  Maybe I'll be dropping this
architecture course.

What is the feasibility of truly open frameworks that mimic the functionality
of .NET becoming available?

I've been looking for a year for a desktop machine that meets all my
criteria: small and quiet; 64-bit; supports virualization; dual core.
Looking a lot.  I haven't found any vendor, or retailer, that can tell
me if their system supports Linux.  I can't find blogs or newsgroups
where anybody discusses having tried running Linux on any of these
systems.  I can't even find that information on motherboards I want to
buy, from high-end retailers.  They don't actually stock any of this
stuff, and so they don't know any more about it (Linux support, or
even if the bios enables VMX) than I can get from the manu's website.

So the only thing to do is research as best I can, then for the
remaining systems try to find a retailer that will let me return it if
it doesn't work.  At Fry's, BTW, you ask them if it runs Linux and
they just tell you to bring it back if it doesn't.  Their return
policy substitutes for sales people having a clue.

If you know of a very small, quiet, 64-bit, dual-core system that
works "almost 100 percent" with Linux and doesn't disable the VMX bit,
please let me know where to order it and I will.

(I think Dell's smallest "Optiplex 745" fills the bill, but it's $400
more than the HP, so the HP was worth a try.)

Oh, and to another reponse about HP using unsupported parts: I think
that may apply less to their business-grade machines.  With those they
seem aware that Linux support is a good thing.

From ianburrell at gmail.com  Thu Apr  5 10:50:40 2007
From: ianburrell at gmail.com (Ian Burrell)
Date: Thu, 5 Apr 2007 10:50:40 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] MONO curiousity...
In-Reply-To: <20070405001356.f88r0bofd44s8ww0@web.robinson-west.com>
References: <20070405001356.f88r0bofd44s8ww0@web.robinson-west.com>
Message-ID: <d91f09cd0704051050v50509396jcbe1aad74f5fbf2@mail.gmail.com>

On 4/5/07, plug_0 at robinson-west.com <plug_0 at robinson-west.com> wrote:
>
> This mono project sounds interesting, except for the closed libraries part.
>

The Mono class libraries are licensed under the MIT X11 license.  That
is open source. The compiler is GPL.  The runtime libraries are LGPL.
The class libraries used to be LGPL before they were relicensed.  The
reasoning what that there is some question how "derived works" is
defined for object-oriented dynamic languages.  They want to allow
people to write closed-source applications in Mono which link
dynamically with the class libraries.

The MIT X11 license does allow people to not release their
modifications but that doesn't seem to be a major danger with a
project where the rest of the code is under the GPL or LGPL.  And
where there is a strong open developer community which will keep the
open-source version moving faster than any proprietary fork.  Other
BSD licensed projects, like BSD, PostgreSQL, or X11 have had
proprietary forks which have been outdone by the open-source version.

 - Ian

Uh yeah, quiet, it's quiet! I was in the same boat as you with a older
not so nice system.  It had a hauppauge card that I got the remote
working fine.  I moved it into the garage and connected it up to cable
and now it works as a seamless secondary server.  This HTPC box comes
with a working serial IR port built into the front.  I set it up to work
with the hauppauge remote that I already had working and it's worked
ever sense.

I have a Sejin IR keyboard that I'd really like to get working with the
IR port, but I'm not patient enough to go through and map every key and
combination key stroke that would be necessary.

cat /proc/cpuinfo
processor       : 0
vendor_id       : AuthenticAMD
cpu family      : 15
model           : 79
model name      : AMD Sempron(tm) Processor 3200+
stepping        : 2
cpu MHz         : 1803.792
cache size      : 128 KB
fpu             : yes
fpu_exception   : yes
cpuid level     : 1
wp              : yes
flags           : fpu vme de pse tsc msr pae mce cx8 apic sep mtrr pge
mca cmov pat pse36 clflush mmx fxsr sse sse2 syscall nx mmxext fxsr_opt
rdtscp lm 3dnowext 3dnow pni cx16 lahf_lm cr8_legacy
bogomips        : 3610.74
TLB size        : 1024 4K pages
clflush size    : 64
cache_alignment : 64
address sizes   : 40 bits physical, 48 bits virtual
power management: ts fid vid ttp tm stc


Although I never always trust these numbers because it does some cpu
frequency changes when it needs to....  With this CPU I can watch a xvid
avi file and record a movie all at the same time....  I've never had any
type of performance hiccup with this setup.

From larry.brigman at gmail.com  Thu Apr  5 21:42:04 2007
From: larry.brigman at gmail.com (Larry Brigman)
Date: Thu, 5 Apr 2007 21:42:04 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] SOL/IPMI
In-Reply-To: <alpine.OSX.0.82.0703121423190.268@arrowhead.galois.com>
References: <alpine.OSX.0.82.0703121423190.268@arrowhead.galois.com>
Message-ID: <ca5165530704052142jc0f87d6r6c52bf3c0e66ec50@mail.gmail.com>

On 3/12/07, Paul Heinlein <heinlein at madboa.com> wrote:
> My little 16-port serial terminal is nearing capacity. I'm willing to
> get another one, but I thought I'd investigate the Serial-over-LAN
> stuff included with IPMIv2.0.
>
> Anyone used it? Battle scars? Successes? Hints?

We are just getting started using this but it is typically a system
hardware support
option.  There is a BMC which can be included on the board or may be part of the
card cage that the boards plug into.

If the hardware you already have doesn't support it then it is not
worth the effort unless
you are planning major upgrades.

This is mainly for racks of headless systems.  We have an six slot
ATCA chassis that
has two shelf managers which support IPMI 1.5 with some form of serial
re-direct but
does not support Serial over LAN.

Here is a link I googled a few minutes ago.
http://buttersideup.com/docs/howto/IPMI_on_Debian.html


From heinlein at madboa.com  Fri Apr  6 07:35:10 2007
From: heinlein at madboa.com (Paul Heinlein)
Date: Fri, 6 Apr 2007 07:35:10 -0700 (PDT)
Subject: [PLUG] SOL/IPMI
In-Reply-To: <ca5165530704052142jc0f87d6r6c52bf3c0e66ec50@mail.gmail.com>
References: <alpine.OSX.0.82.0703121423190.268@arrowhead.galois.com>
	<ca5165530704052142jc0f87d6r6c52bf3c0e66ec50@mail.gmail.com>
Message-ID: <alpine.OSX.0.83.0704060715070.12172@arrowhead.galois.com>

On Thu, 5 Apr 2007, Larry Brigman wrote:

> On 3/12/07, Paul Heinlein <heinlein at madboa.com> wrote:
>>  My little 16-port serial terminal is nearing capacity. I'm willing to
>>  get another one, but I thought I'd investigate the Serial-over-LAN
>>  stuff included with IPMIv2.0.
>>
>>  Anyone used it? Battle scars? Successes? Hints?
>
> We are just getting started using this but it is typically a system 
> hardware support option.  There is a BMC which can be included on 
> the board or may be part of the card cage that the boards plug into.

The machines in question, mostly from Penguin Computing, have BMCs 
with their own (unswitched) connections to the power supplies and 
ethernet jacks.

> This is mainly for racks of headless systems.  We have an six slot 
> ATCA chassis that has two shelf managers which support IPMI 1.5 with 
> some form of serial re-direct but does not support Serial over LAN.

Right. The docs I've been able to find online seem to warn against 
using the (mostly busted) serial support in IPMI 1.5. Supposedly, 
things are better in 2.0, but HOWTOs are in short supply.

> Here is a link I googled a few minutes ago.
> http://buttersideup.com/docs/howto/IPMI_on_Debian.html

That was one of the first documents I read. It's pretty specific to a 
certain chipset.

I've installed the OpenIPMI stuff and have been able to poll the 
hardware monitors with ipmitool, e.g.,

   [root]# ipmitool sdr type Temperature
   CPU 1 TEMP       | 22h | ok  |  3.0 | 48 degrees C
   CPU 2 TEMP       | 23h | ok  |  3.1 | 49 degrees C
   MEM TEMP         | 24h | ok  |  7.0 | 27 degrees C
   PWR CONN TEMP    | 25h | ok  |  7.0 | 33 degrees C

I've even been able to locate the proper LAN channels on the machines 
in question (oddly, one does lan on channel 6, while others on are 
channel 2).

I'm more interested in how folks use IPMI as a console interface for 
rebooting, altering BIOS settings, and getting to the boot loader.

-- 
Paul Heinlein <> heinlein at madboa.com <> http://www.madboa.com/

Subject: lirc and mythtv (Was: Re: [PLUG] Linux on HP Slimline s7700n;
	Fry's returns)
In-Reply-To: <461540E8.3080408@schluting.com>
References: <20070405161028.99DD74314@ulke.nakedape.cc>
	<20070405162927.GN2770@amethyst.spiritone.com>	<46152FE6.9020607@gmail.com>
	<1175797597.8323.9.camel@willow.odshp.com>
	<461540E8.3080408@schluting.com>
Message-ID: <1175877949.15377.10.camel@willow.odshp.com>

On Thu, 2007-04-05 at 11:33 -0700, Charlie Schluting wrote:

> MythTV simply rocks, save the extreme crappiness of lirc. Gawd. I'm very
> tempted to just buy a new remote (people report that certain lirc
> modules work fine with 2.6.20).

Have you tried using the kernel event interface instead of the default?
I ran lircd with '-H dev/input -d /dev/input/eventX' and was able to run
'irw' without making lircd die a painful death, and made a test config
with a few keys with 'irrecord'.  (Well, actually, 'irw' doesn't
actually record anything when lircd is run this way, but 'irrecord'
does.)

The funny thing, though, is that when I run lircd w/o options or without
it running, the arrows, "ok" and number pad works, but none of the
others do.  My vague understanding is that it's using the (new-ish)
kernel input interface (which is what /dev/input/* stuff is), so the
remote acts like a keyboard, but a bunch of keys that aren't actually
mapped.  I can actually run xev and--even stranger--*most* of the keys
generate X events.

Wil

From wamorita at hevanet.com  Sun Apr  8 07:16:38 2007
From: wamorita at hevanet.com (William A Morita)
Date: Sun, 8 Apr 2007 07:16:38 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Linux Fund loses its funding source
Message-ID: <006e01c779e8$86784130$6d01a8c0@RAMA>

http://community.linux.com/community/07/04/02/1356254.shtml?tid=11

ChangeLog: Linux Fund loses its funding source


Thursday April 05, 2007 (01:54 PM GMT)

By: Linux.com staff

Linux Fund <http://www.linuxfund.org/> , an organization founded to fund
open source projects through financing from affinity credit cards, is losing
its affinity card provider. Linux Fund cardholders received official notice
from the Bank of America recently that the program is being discontinued.
According to the letter, "the provider of your Linux Fund credit card and
Linux Fund recently agreed to discontinue the existing credit card program
effective June 30, 2007." 

David Mandel, executive director of LinuxFund.org, says, "Linux Fund and
Bank of America (formerly MBNA) agreed to terminate the Linux Fund card a
few months ago, but we could not say anything because of non-disclosure
agreements we signed with MBNA.

"Many people have asked if Linux Fund is going to offer a card through
another bank or if I know of an alternative to the Linux Fund credit card
that funds FOSS. I cannot say anything about Linux Fund's future with any
other bank; and I do not know of any alternative to the Linux Fund credit
card. I suspect there aren't any. I can say that the banking industry has
changed and it is much more difficult to set up a program like the Linux
Fund card than it was in 1999 when we started the program."

LinuxFund was born in 1999 and hyped at the 2000 LinuxWorld in New York
City. After falling into a period of disarray and neglect, it was
revitalized <http://business.newsforge.com/article.pl?sid=05/07/22/1553240>
in 2005, and channeled funds into important FOSS projects such as Blender,
Debian Linux, Gentoo, and OpenSSH <http://www.linuxfund.org/1/projects.php>
.

"Linux Fund will continue to exist, although it will look very different
than it does today," Mandel says. "Linux Fund is hoping to be able to
provide the open source community with the information and tools they need
to intelligently channel funds and other resources to open source projects."


- Bill Morita
wamorita at hevanet.com

Rod,

   Bank of America had a small credit card business. They bought MBNA because
that company was the largest credit card issuer in the country. I read how
many different brands of VISA and MasterCard they issued, but forget the
number. It was very large.

   BoA is one of the huge corporations that will cut all small pieces that
appear to cost more to maintain then they produce in revenues. What a group
like Linux Fund needs is a small bank -- local or regional -- that can see
the benefits of sponsoring such a branded card.

From jeme at brelin.net  Sun Apr  8 17:24:00 2007
From: jeme at brelin.net (Jeme A Brelin)
Date: Sun, 8 Apr 2007 17:24:00 -0700 (PDT)

On Sun, 8 Apr 2007, Rich Shepard wrote:
>  BoA is one of the huge corporations that will cut all small pieces that 
> appear to cost more to maintain then they produce in revenues. What a 
> group like Linux Fund needs is a small bank -- local or regional -- that 
> can see the benefits of sponsoring such a branded card.

In fact, a corporation like BofA will cut a piece of business that is 
certainly producing more revenue than it costs to maintain if that piece 
of business does not have a high enough profit margin.  Moreover, the 
corporation would be doing its shareholders a disservice if it failed to 
cut those pieces of business (and potentially opens itself up to lawsuits 
from its own shareholders for such failures).  The system rewards high 
profit MARGINS, not high profits.  A business that's making plenty of 
money can still be seen as worthless to the stock market if it does so on 
low margins, no matter how consistently.

People who play games are way smarter than people who make games (because 
there are so many MORE of them).  The rules might be set up with the 
intention of promoting a certain type of behavior, but people will find 
what behavior is actually rewarded and act that way.

J.
--

      Jeme A Brelin
     jeme at brelin.net

From wamorita at hevanet.com  Tue Apr 10 10:14:22 2007
From: wamorita at hevanet.com (William A Morita)
Date: Tue, 10 Apr 2007 10:14:22 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Sunspots reaching 1,000-year high
Message-ID: <000001c77b93$afe26fd0$6d01a8c0@RAMA>

http://news.bbc.co.uk/2/hi/science/nature/3869753.stm
 Sunspots reaching 1,000-year high
By Dr David Whitehouse
BBC News Online science editor

A new analysis shows that the Sun is more active now than it has been at
anytime in the previous 1,000 years.

Scientists based at the Institute for Astronomy in Zurich used ice cores
from Greenland to construct a picture of our star's activity in the past.

They say that over the last century the number of sunspots rose at the same
time that the Earth's climate became steadily warmer.

This trend is being amplified by gases from fossil fuel burning, they argue.

'Little Ice Age'

Sunspots have been monitored on the Sun since 1610, shortly after the
invention of the telescope. They provide the longest-running direct
measurement of our star's activity.

The variation in sunspot numbers has revealed the Sun's 11-year cycle of
activity as well as other, longer-term changes.

In particular, it has been noted that between about 1645 and 1715, few
sunspots were seen on the Sun's surface.

This period is called the Maunder Minimum after the English astronomer who
studied it.

It coincided with a spell of prolonged cold weather often referred to as the
"Little Ice Age". Solar scientists strongly suspect there is a link between
the two events - but the exact mechanism remains elusive.

Over the past few thousand years there is evidence of earlier Maunder-like
coolings in the Earth's climate - indicated by tree-ring measurements that
show slow growth due to prolonged cold.

In an attempt to determine what happened to sunspots during these other cold
periods, Dr Sami Solanki and colleagues have looked at concentrations of a
form, or isotope, of beryllium in ice cores from Greenland.

The isotope is created by cosmic rays - high-energy particles from the
depths of the galaxy.

The flux of cosmic rays reaching the Earth's surface is modulated by the
strength of the solar wind, the charged particles that stream away from the
Sun's surface.

And since the strength of the solar wind varies over the sunspot cycle, the
amount of beryllium in the ice at a time in the past can therefore be used
to infer the state of the Sun and, roughly, the number of sunspots.

Latest warming

Dr Solanki is presenting a paper on the reconstruction of past solar
activity at Cool Stars, Stellar Systems And The Sun, a conference in
Hamburg, Germany.

He says that the reconstruction shows the Maunder Minimum and the other
minima that are known in the past thousand years.

But the most striking feature, he says, is that looking at the past 1,150
years the Sun has never been as active as it has been during the past 60
years.

Over the past few hundred years, there has been a steady increase in the
numbers of sunspots, a trend that has accelerated in the past century, just
at the time when the Earth has been getting warmer.

The data suggests that changing solar activity is influencing in some way
the global climate causing the world to get warmer.

Over the past 20 years, however, the number of sunspots has remained roughly
constant, yet the average temperature of the Earth has continued to
increase.

This is put down to a human-produced greenhouse effect caused by the
combustion of fossil fuels.

This latest analysis shows that the Sun has had a considerable indirect
influence on the global climate in the past, causing the Earth to warm or
chill, and that mankind is amplifying the Sun's latest attempt to warm the
Earth.

Story from BBC NEWS:
http://news.bbc.co.uk/go/pr/fr/-/2/hi/science/nature/3869753.stm

Published: 2004/07/06 16:07:14 GMT

C BBC MMVII

- Bill Morita 
wamorita at hevanet.com 





From plug_0 at robinson-west.com  Tue Apr 10 03:51:58 2007
From: plug_0 at robinson-west.com (plug_0 at robinson-west.com)
Date: Tue, 10 Apr 2007 03:51:58 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Special purpose email use...
Message-ID: <20070410035158.58tc1shuogcw4ksw@xerxes.robinson-west.com>

Here is a set of procmail recipes I've developed:

:0:
* ^X-Original-To: servermsg\@robinson-west\.com
* !^Subject: router hostname
/dev/null

:0:
* ^X-Original-To: servermsg\@robinson-west\.com
* web.robinson-west.com \[192.168.1.1\]
* Received: from web.robinson-west.com
/home/admin/mail/servermsg

:0:
* ^X-Original-To: servermsg\@robinson-west\.com
* xerxes.robinson-west.com \[192.168.1.4\]
* Received: from xerxes.robinson-west.com
/home/admin/mail/servermsg

:0:
* ^X-Original-To: servermsg\@robinson-west\.com
/dev/null

------------------------------------------------

I'm trying to use procmail to set up a filter that will only
accept email to a specific alias that has a specific subject
from specific sources.  How do my recipes look so far?

I want to require that the body contain a single string that is
a fully qualified domain name, but I don't think procmail can
check that for me.

I'm trying to experiment with sending messages between servers
via email that ultimately should be acted on in an automated
way.  I'm looking at use of my local email system as a possible
alternative to using EasyTCP for specialized intraserver
communication.  The smtp protocol is well defined, it's capable
of retrying if the first attempt to send a message fails,
etcetera.

One application I'm considering is special email addresses
on a backup email server that it only accept messages to from the
primary email server.  The primary and backup server could synchronise:
grey lists, white lists, etcetera through this special, not available
to the public, email address.  Another thought of mine is simply
keeping track of whether or not my partner smtp server is alive
so that I can soft reject messages for it when it is still up
and running.

Out of curiosity, is there any way to comment procmail files?

In general these days, is the standard across the Net to interpret
multiple MX records as a load balancing mechanism instead of as a
linear list by weight of servers to try in a specific order?

Michael Robinson

On Tue, 10 Apr 2007 09:13:46 -0700
Mel Andres <mel97215 at comcast.net> dijo:

> John Jason Jordan wrote:
> > My router is dying. It's a Linksys 10/100 without a firewall. For a
> > replacement I'd like gigabit and a firewall. I just got back from Fry's
> > where I bought a Linksys RVS400 for $129 that turned out to be DOA.
> > Since my dying router is also Linksys I am currently not impressed with
> > Linksys quality. 

> I found support from Linksys to be problematic. They are tied to a 
> windows based script, and insist on using the GUI.
> After three iterations, of warranty based support calls, I bought a 
> Belkin. Not necessarily cheap, but been happier ever since.

It took the better part of today, but I am back online. 

The first thing I did was go to Free Geek to get a new plain 10/100
router. Unfortunately, I arrove too early and they weren't open yet, so
I killed some time going to City Liqudators and a couple other places
checking out storage cabinets for the clinic. (Disappointing results.)
Finally at Free Geek I bought a D-Link and a Linksys for $15 each.

When I got them home I couldn't get either one to work. I spent at
least an hour poking at them with every trick I knew of, but no way
could I get on the internet. When my frustration level got too high I
packed them up and took them back to Free Geek, along with my laptop. A
kind soul from the back room got the configuration page up on each one
in Firefox, and also showed me how to use a paper clip to reset them to
factory defaults, in case I got the router so goobered up that it
wouldn't even respond to a ping. Another gentleman was watching and
added the suggestion that Comcast likes to block routers by locking a
router's MAC address into the modem so the modem won't let traffic
through from any other device. (This sounded a bit phony to me, but
what do I know?)

So back at home I tried the Linksys first. While it worked at Free
Geek, back at home I couldn't even get the configuration page up on the
browser. Eventually I hooked my Ubuntu laptop directly to the cable
modem so I could at least get on the net, then went to linksys.com and
found a tech support number. The call to tech support was taken by a
young lady whose English pronunciation was terrible. Add that the phone
connection was spitting static on every word, and I ended up having to
ask her to repeat every sentence, sometimes two or three times. Finally
she insisted that she could not help unless I was running Windows, so I
booted my Windows 2000 desktop. She asked me to type ipconfig at a
command line, which gave an ip address of 169.something. It was an
address I had never seen before. I tried to get her to explain what it
meant, but the communication problem made it impossible for me to
understand her. After a few more minutes she asked for the serial
number of the router. When I gave it to her she said it was "past its
expired of warranty out," but that she could give me a number for their
Premium Support Service, which would cost me only $29.50 for the first
five minutes and $5 a minute thereafter. I hung up on her. Anyone need
a Linksys router?

At that point I plugged in the D-Link. Again, no matter what I did, I
could not get on the net. Finally I called Comcast to see if the second
gentleman's opinion was correct. A helpful fellow (native English
speaker with a good phone connection!) spent 15 minutes with me, but
couldn't get it working. He did say that the only MAC address Comcast
looks at is the modem's MAC address and that, unless I changed the
modem, there should be no problem. Just in case, I got him to give me
the MAC address of my modem, which I now have taped to the side of its
plastic housing.

After hanging up with him I shut down the Windows 2000 desktop, took
the cables for the three computers off the separate 8-port switch where
they normally live and put them directly on the router, then went to my
Ubuntu laptop. On the laptop I was finally able to get the
configuration page up in Firefox for the D-Link, although I still
couldn't go anywhere on the internet. I poked and poked and then,
suddenly, it worked!! Unfortunately, I have no idea why it suddenly
decided to work. That is bad, because I like to document which specific
poke fixed something for next time I break it. 

Now I need to put the cables back on the 8-port switch because the
router has only four ports + modem, and I have three computers and four
laser printers. But I'll tackle that later. For now I'm just happy to
have a router that works.


From alan at clueserver.org  Tue Apr 10 19:33:00 2007
From: alan at clueserver.org (Alan)
Date: Tue, 10 Apr 2007 19:33:00 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] PLUG Advanced Topics Meeting April 16th 2007 7PM
Message-ID: <1176258780.16977.8.camel@zowie.fnordora.org>

PLUG Advanced Topics Meeting 
April 16th 2007 at 7pm

Location:

Jax Bar and Grill
826 SW 2nd Avenue 
Portland, Oregon

Speaker:  Josh Triplett <josh at freedesktop.org>

At the April 16th Advanced Topics meeting, Josh will discuss Sparse,
static analysis, and its application to synchronization techniques, with
an emphasis on Read-Copy Update (RCU).  I will also give a brief history
of how I ended up maintaining Sparse.

About Josh Triplett:

I study at Portland State University, in the second year of the CS PhD
program.  My research relates to advanced synchronization techniques
based on "relativistic programming", which avoids the expensive illusion
of an absolute view of memory across multiple processors.  I hack on
various Free and Open Source projects for fun, study, and profit.  I
participate in the Debian project, in freedesktop.org, in the Linux
kernel community, and in the Portland State Aerospace Society.  As of
2006, I maintain the Sparse static analysis tool for C, originally
written by Linus Torvalds.

Usual meeting rules apply.  Happy Hour menu for the first hour for food.


-- 
Alan <alan at clueserver.org>

If Comcast is anything like Verizon, DHCP will only assign one address
per port.  The only
way to get things to allow something else to start up is either do a
DHCP release from
the equipment with the MAC address that was issued the IP address or
wait until 2X the
DHCP lease time before trying to connect something else.  Had the same
issue with
Verizon when I was upgrading my router.  After the third tech call
about the same problem
I ended up with either a second or third level support person or
someone at first level that
really should be at second or third level that told me how the system worked.
Since my router didn't have the release function, I left it off for an
hour and then
everything connected up.

So the guy at FG was probably right about Comcast.

Subject: [PLUG] Strange X problem
Message-ID: <621815.93438.qm@web50606.mail.re2.yahoo.com>

The two distros I have used most recently are SuSE 10.0 and 10.2. These installations have been partitioned as / and /home.

I create non-root accounts on Linux, which of course is a recommended practice. Suddenly it becomes impossible to obtain an X session with a non-root account, but if a different non-root account is created, it will work for awhile before the same thing happens. It is always possible to obtain an X session with a root login, but then you're no more secure than you would be with Windows. As a matter of fact, that's what I'm doing right now (logged in as root).

Unless I get some insight into why this happens, I'm going to use a different distro. Fedora comes to mind, but it has frightening reviews:

http://www.softwareinreview.com/cms/content/view/57

No, probably Debian. But I'm curious as to why the problem I mentioned above is SuSE-specific, seemingly.

Subject: [PLUG] DNS mystery...
Message-ID: <20070410180943.9esoa0bg0swk4kko@xerxes.robinson-west.com>

The mail server for sophistasis.com is supposed to be the backup exchanger
for robinson-west.com.  Apparently, my brother got a message meant for me
and should have been able to check my name server for my server's address,
but he got the ip for sophistasis instead.

Okay, what happened I wonder???


   /-------------\                  /--------\
   | sophistasis |-----Internet-----| xerxes |
   \-------------/                  \--------/

* Did a spammer send the message and simultaneously manage to
poison the DNS server on web?

* Is a renegade gateway responsible, possibly inside Opus?

* Is there a weakness in the iBook my brother isn't aware
of which allows a remote site to change dns response
packets?

I've noticed that I can't get register.com to accept ns1.sophistasis.com
as a valid name server for robinson-west.com.  I don't know why that
is.  I remember a dns test suggested that Opus is running a vulnerable
release of bind where I am using Opus's servers as a tertiary option.
Oddly and irritatingly, the register.com domain tools show the Opus
server as the first server in my DNS list.

Most routers or modems don't work immediately after plugging them in.  I
have noticed delays of 10-15 minutes sometimes.  I think it relates to
how often the wan responds or polls the routers.

I have also noticed that I often have to restart my networking after
connecting to a router, so it will request and receive an address from
the router, before I can connect to the configuration page.

> Geek, back at home I couldn't even get the configuration page up on the
> browser. Eventually I hooked my Ubuntu laptop directly to the cable
> modem so I could at least get on the net, then went to linksys.com and
> found a tech support number. The call to tech support was taken by a
> young lady whose English pronunciation was terrible. Add that the phone
> connection was spitting static on every word, and I ended up having to
> ask her to repeat every sentence, sometimes two or three times. Finally
> she insisted that she could not help unless I was running Windows, so I
> booted my Windows 2000 desktop. She asked me to type ipconfig at a
> command line, which gave an ip address of 169.something. It was an
> address I had never seen before. I tried to get her to explain what it
> meant, but the communication problem made it impossible for me to
> understand her. After a few more minutes she asked for the serial
> number of the router. When I gave it to her she said it was "past its
> expired of warranty out," but that she could give me a number for their
> Premium Support Service, which would cost me only $29.50 for the first
> five minutes and $5 a minute thereafter. I hung up on her. Anyone need
> a Linksys router?
>
> At that point I plugged in the D-Link. Again, no matter what I did, I
> could not get on the net. Finally I called Comcast to see if the second
> gentleman's opinion was correct. A helpful fellow (native English
> speaker with a good phone connection!) spent 15 minutes with me, but
> couldn't get it working. He did say that the only MAC address Comcast
> looks at is the modem's MAC address and that, unless I changed the
> modem, there should be no problem. Just in case, I got him to give me
> the MAC address of my modem, which I now have taped to the side of its
> plastic housing.
>
> After hanging up with him I shut down the Windows 2000 desktop, took
> the cables for the three computers off the separate 8-port switch where
> they normally live and put them directly on the router, then went to my
> Ubuntu laptop. On the laptop I was finally able to get the
> configuration page up in Firefox for the D-Link, although I still
> couldn't go anywhere on the internet. I poked and poked and then,
> suddenly, it worked!! Unfortunately, I have no idea why it suddenly
> decided to work. That is bad, because I like to document which specific
> poke fixed something for next time I break it. 
>
> Now I need to put the cables back on the 8-port switch because the
> router has only four ports + modem, and I have three computers and four
> laser printers. But I'll tackle that later. For now I'm just happy to
> have a router that works.
> _______________________________________________
> PLUG mailing list
> PLUG at lists.pdxlinux.org
> http://lists.pdxlinux.org/mailman/listinfo/plug
>
>   
The mystery with the Linksys was solved. Once I had the Belkin up and 
running, I tossed it into a closet and forgot about it for awhile. 
Later, my son-in-law started working for Comcast, and I gave it to him. 
My problem was that the wireless would work, but the gateway failed to 
connect us to the internet. Linksys support sent me two replacements and 
couldn't get them to work. Alex, opened up a command prompt, instead of 
using the GUI.
With an ipconfig /release; ipconfig /renew - all was working normally. 
Of course, I wondered why I hadn't thought of that myself. But then, 
neither did advanced tech support at Linksys. There was no need to clone 
a MAC address either.

Mel


From rsteff at comcast.net  Wed Apr 11 14:23:15 2007
From: rsteff at comcast.net (Richard C. Steffens)
Date: Wed, 11 Apr 2007 14:23:15 -0700
Subject: Resolved: [PLUG] Starting a Shell Script from Konqueror's File Browser
In-Reply-To: <1175555203.13172.44.camel@atlas>
References: <460A8E58.3070108@comcast.net>	<200703282222.35167.ewilhelm@cpan.org>
	<460C25A0.4020800@comcast.net>	<200703291649.26739.ewilhelm@cpan.org>
	<1175555203.13172.44.camel@atlas>
Message-ID: <461D51C3.2000104@comcast.net>

The original question was, how do I get a shell script to execute when I
click on it in the Konqueror file browser. It turns out that it does
execute, but it assumes you want it to refer to files and directories
that are in your home directory, not the directory in which the script
is located.

I joined the KDE forum and got a lead on the answer. It is a bit of a
kludge, but here's how to create a click-able entry in a directory that
will execute a shell script in that directory on files in that directory:

Create a shell script, such as MyShellScript.
In Konqueror, create a link to the shell script:
    Right click on the empty space in the Konqueror folder contents
    pane and select Create New > Link to Application...
    Give the link an appropriate name such as "Link to MyShellScript"
    At the Application tab of the Link Properties window find the line
      labeled Command:, click "Browse" and, once you've browsed to it,
      select MyShellScript.
    At the line labeled Work Path: click on the "Open file dialog" icon,
      make sure the dialog has the correct directory highlighted, and
      click OK.
    Finally, click OK on the Link Properties window.

Ken Stephens wrote:

> Just wondering if you could associate the .sh to an xterm -c $0 so
> that it would open a terminal window and you could see what it is
> doing. Also see any errors you may be getting.

As an additional feature, if you are having trouble debugging your shell
script, you can click on Advanced Options on the Link Properties window,
check the boxes, "Run in terminal" and "Do not close when command exits".

-- 
Regards,

Dick Steffens

> The original question was, how do I get a shell script to execute when I

> execute, but it assumes you want it to refer to files and directories

> kludge, but here's how to create a click-able entry in a directory that
> will execute a shell script in that directory on files in that directory:
>
> [snip]

It's possible to solve this in the script by having the script
determine it's own location and then cd'ing to it.

  #!/bin/sh
  self=`which $0`
  mydir=${self%/*}
  cd $mydir

Terry

   cd $(dirname $0)

From johnxj at comcast.net  Thu Apr 12 18:24:09 2007
From: johnxj at comcast.net (John Jason Jordan)
Date: Thu, 12 Apr 2007 18:24:09 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Routers again <sigh>
Message-ID: <20070412182409.93927b2c.johnxj@comcast.net>

To recap old history, my Linksys router died. I replaced it with a
D-Link from Free Geek. The Linksys had five ports on the back, one
labeled Modem, and the others numbered 1-4. The new D-Link is the same,
except that the one marked Modem on the Linksys is marked WAN on the
D-Link. The cable modem is in the WAN port on the new D-Link. There is
also a cable going from 1 on the router to a separate 8-port switch.
Cables from the switch go to my three computers and two laser
printers. This is also how my Linksys was cabled, and everything always
worked.

Now, with the new D-Link all three computers can go to the internet
without a problem. But none of the computers can talk to either laser
printer. 

The laser printers are 192.168.1.4 and 192.168.1.24. When I turn each
one on the corresponding light on the front of the switch lights up. So
the wiring is still connected.

If I try to print to either one the print job just sits in the queue in
CUPS. Ditto for trying to print a test page. If I ping the printers
from the Edgy laptop I get:

jjj at Devil5:~$ ping 192.168.1.4
PING 192.168.1.4 (192.168.1.4) 56(84) bytes of data.
>From 192.168.1.3 icmp_seq=2 Destination Host Unreachable

>From the Fedora 7 test2 desktop I get

[jjj at localhost -]$ ping 192.168.1.4
PING 192.168.1.4 (192.168.1.4) 56(84) bytes of data.
	and then the ping command just hangs

While testing I have Firestarter on the laptop and the firewall and
SELinux on the Fedora desktop all disabled.

My first question is, what does this line mean?

>From 192.168.1.3 icmp_seq=2 Destination Host Unreachable

And consider that 192.168.1.3 is the Windows 2000 desktop. It is turned
off, as it usually is. However, with the cable connecting its ethernet
card to the switch plugged in, the light on the switch is on. How can
that be if the computer is turned off? And I can ping the Windows
deslktop  from the Ubuntu laptop and get a response, even if the
Windows desktop is turned off, but from the Fedora 7 desktop the ping
just hangs.

Summary: The D-Link is wired exactly the same as the old Linksys was
wired. With the Linksys any computer could print to either printer
whether it was the only computer turned on or if all the computers were
running. Now nothing can talk to the printers. The only thing that is
different in the mix is the D-Link router. Therefore, that is where the
problem must lie. But what could it be?

The computer with the nic connection on when the computer is off has remote
power on enabled in the BIOS to allow remote admin update.  This is a
feature that
most motherboards have now.

Were the printers configured for DHCP?  The new router might have
re-assigned them.
Have you checked the router page for known hosts/MACs?
Can you ping the other computers?
Have you tried
ping -b 192.168.1.0 from one of your Linux boxes?

On Thu, 12 Apr 2007 18:48:04 -0700
"Larry Brigman" <larry.brigman at gmail.com> dijo:

> The computer with the nic connection on when the computer is off has remote
> power on enabled in the BIOS to allow remote admin update.  This is a
> feature that most motherboards have now.

Thanks. That explains one part of the mystery.

> Were the printers configured for DHCP?  The new router might have
> re-assigned them.
> Have you checked the router page for known hosts/MACs?
> Can you ping the other computers?
> Have you tried
> ping -b 192.168.1.0 from one of your Linux boxes?

This is what I got:

root at Devil5:/home/jjj# ping -b 192.168.1.4
PING 192.168.1.4 (192.168.1.4) 56(84) bytes of data.
ping: sendmsg: Operation not permitted
ping: sendmsg: Operation not permitted

However, then I disabled Firestarter, which gave me:

root at Devil5:/home/jjj# ping -b 192.168.1.4
PING 192.168.1.4 (192.168.1.4) 56(84) bytes of data.
>From 192.168.1.3 icmp_seq=2 Destination Host Unreachable

Note that I did sudo su to become a real root first. But also note the
results of the command after disabling Firestarter on the laptop. What
I don't understand is why it says "from 192.168.1.3 ..."  That is the
Windows 2000 desktop, which is turned off except for the echo I am
getting from its network card.

After the above I decided to take the laptop over to the Laserjet and
use a patch cord to connect it directly to the printer. Again, print
jobs just stalled and went nowhere; the lights on the printer did not
start blinking. When I pinged it I got the same "operation not
permitted." Thus it is now clear that the router has nothing to do with
it. Something on this laptop is causing the problem.

So then I decided to try the new Fedora 7 test2 desktop computer. I
tried using it to ping 192.168.1.4 and the ping command just hung,
instead of giving me the "operation not permitted" error message. The
printer was turned on and connected to the network, but I had not set
up this printer yet in CUPS on the new desktop, so I went ahead and did
so. I tried everything, but I could not get it to print a test page.

I had already set up the other laser printer in the new desktop
computer, so I fiddled with it a bit, but was also unable to get a test
page out of it. Nor could I ping it -- like the first laser printer,
the ping command just hung.

However, I noticed one thing on the new desktop computer -- when I told
the GUI to print a test page for either printer a little window popped
up and said "printing test page as job 7," or some other job number.
Now, if there is a job that is hung I am not going to get any output
from the printer until I clear the hung job. Unfortunately, I poked all
over Fedora 7 and absolutely cannot find any way to bring up the CUPS
GUI where I can open a print jobs dialog box. CUPS is definitely
installed and running, but perhaps F7 test2 does not install the CUPS
GUI. If there is a command line way to find out what jobs are currently
in the queue, I don't know what it is. If there are any Fedora users
here, maybe someone can tell me where they hid the button to launch the
GUI.

And anyway, even if there are jobs hung in the queue, I should still
get a ping response.

Another fact is that I remember installing an upgrade for CUPS a few
days ago.

So far all I know for sure is that the problem cannot be in the router
because connecting the computer directly to the printer doesn't work
either. Otherwise I am stumped.

I have a homework I needed to print out for class tomorrow morning, but
I can print it out at the university computer lab before class.
Nevertheless, getting my printers working is a high priority.

          inet addr:192.168.0.100  Bcast:192.168.0.255
Mask:255.255.255.0 inet6 addr: fe80::20f:b0ff:fe03:386/64 Scope:Link
          UP BROADCAST RUNNING MULTICAST  MTU:1500  Metric:1

          RX bytes:135886534 (129.5 MiB)  TX bytes:10454916 (9.9 MiB)

>From the desktop I get the same thing with a few details different:

eth0      Link encap:Ethernet  HWaddr 00:90:27:76:88:86:8D
          inet addr:192.168.0.101  Bcast:192.168.0.255
Mask:255.255.255.0 inet6 addr: fe80::290:27ff:fe76:888d/64 Scope:Link
          UP BROADCAST RUNNING MULTICAST  MTU:1500  Metric:1
          RX packets:13281943 errors:0 dropped:0 overruns:0 frame:0
          TX packets: errors:0 dropped:0 overruns:0 carrier:0
          collisions:0 txqueuelen:1000 
          RX bytes:12541853409 (11.6 GiB)  TX bytes:3844030676 (3.5 GiB)

The router is set to 192.168.0.1.

Omigod, I just got it. The "0" in the router address is the subnet. I
just now went into CUPS on the laptop and changed the IP connection
addresses for the printers from 19.168.1.4 and 192.168.1.24 to
192.168.0.4 and 192.168.0.24. And now they both print! Yay!

Some day I may actually understand this stuff.

Thanks!

On 4/12/07, John Jason Jordan <johnxj at comcast.net> wrote:
> On Thu, 12 Apr 2007 18:48:04 -0700
> "Larry Brigman" <larry.brigman at gmail.com> dijo:
>
> > The computer with the nic connection on when the computer is off has remote
> > power on enabled in the BIOS to allow remote admin update.  This is a
> > feature that most motherboards have now.
>
> Thanks. That explains one part of the mystery.
>
> > Were the printers configured for DHCP?  The new router might have
> > re-assigned them.
> > Have you checked the router page for known hosts/MACs?
> > Can you ping the other computers?
> > Have you tried
> > ping -b 192.168.1.0 from one of your Linux boxes?
>
> This is what I got:
>
> root at Devil5:/home/jjj# ping -b 192.168.1.4
> PING 192.168.1.4 (192.168.1.4) 56(84) bytes of data.
> ping: sendmsg: Operation not permitted
> ping: sendmsg: Operation not permitted
>

you missed the point
ping -b 192.168.1.0
is a broadcast ping to the network.  You don't  send it to an address normally.
This should respond back with everything on the network that understands
ping. Lots of duplicates.

I see from the other messages that your new router changed the network
and so you
are up and running.

Larry Brigman wrote:
> ping -b 192.168.1.0
> is a broadcast ping to the network. This should respond back with
> everything on the network that understands ping. Lots of duplicates.

Not everything.  Some IP stacks, on Windows for example, don't respond to
broadcast pings.

On 4/13/07, Michael Rasmussen <mikeraz at patch.com> wrote:
>
> Larry Brigman wrote:
> > ping -b 192.168.1.0
> > is a broadcast ping to the network. This should respond back with
> > everything on the network that understands ping. Lots of duplicates.
>
> Not everything.  Some IP stacks, on Windows for example, don't respond to
> broadcast pings.

Nor should they, given the DDoS amplification potential of broadcast
pings (Smurf attack).  If you need to diagnose between two devices,
use a unicast (standard) ping.  If you need to discover devices on a
subnet, use nmap or some other tool that can automate pinging through
a list of IP addresses.

Linux allows broadcast ping response to be configured through the
net.ipv4.icmp_echo_ignore_broadcasts sysctl, or via the equivalent
/proc/sys/net entry.  The default is to ignore (1).

Jason

automatically, so I use 'user, noauto' options in the fstab entries for

the same for the two Etch partitions.)

But it's not working.  The partitions are being mounted whenever I boot
into one or the other.  What am I doing wrong?

On 4/13/07, Larry Brigman <larry.brigman at gmail.com> wrote:
> On 4/13/07, Jason Martin <nsxfreddy at gmail.com> wrote:
> > On 4/13/07, Michael Rasmussen <mikeraz at patch.com> wrote:
> > >
> > > Larry Brigman wrote:
> > > > ping -b 192.168.1.0
> > > > is a broadcast ping to the network. This should respond back with
> > > > everything on the network that understands ping. Lots of duplicates.
> > >
> > > Not everything.  Some IP stacks, on Windows for example, don't respond to
> > > broadcast pings.
> >
> > Nor should they, given the DDoS amplification potential of broadcast
> > pings (Smurf attack).  If you need to diagnose between two devices,
> > use a unicast (standard) ping.  If you need to discover devices on a
> > subnet, use nmap or some other tool that can automate pinging through
> > a list of IP addresses.
> >
> > Linux allows broadcast ping response to be configured through the
> > net.ipv4.icmp_echo_ignore_broadcasts sysctl, or via the equivalent
> > /proc/sys/net entry.  The default is to ignore (1).
> >
>
> It might be default now but not too many kernel versions ago it was 0
> and would respond.
> However, most networking equipment and devices like networked
> printers/print servers
> will respond.  It is a tool. Useful in some situations.

Looks like the default was changed to disallow broadcast echos in late 2005:

http://git.kernel.org/?p=linux/kernel/git/torvalds/linux-2.6.git;a=commit;h=7ce312467edc270fcbd8a699efabb37ce1802b98

It is a tool.  As I pointed out in my earlier mail, there are
alternatives that rely on the tool itself to generate unicast
echo-requests, instead of broadcasting to everyone.  For example, you
can do an nmap ping scan with the -sP option:

nmap -sP 192.168.1.0/24

The advantage here, is that unlike a broadcast ping, this method
requires the requester to generate 254 individual icmp-echo-request
packets, with the expectation of getting up to 254 icmp-echo-reply
packets back.  With a broadcast ping, the requester sends 1
icmp-echo-request packet and gets up to n icmp-echo-replies, where n
is 254/65534/16777214 for a class C/B/A network respectively.  This
was the smurf technique used to bring down even the largest ISP
backbones from a single dial-up connection.

Jason

Could script it too

#generate a class C list of IP's
for i < 255; echo 192.168.x.$i; done
#export your IP list and then fping it
for i $iplist; do fping $i; done

From johnxj at comcast.net  Fri Apr 13 17:48:16 2007
From: johnxj at comcast.net (John Jason Jordan)
Date: Fri, 13 Apr 2007 17:48:16 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Copying CD/DVD with two drives
Message-ID: <20070413174816.8b2b539a.johnxj@comcast.net>

My new Fedora 7 test3 amd64 desktop has two identical LG DVD+-RW dual
layer drives. I did this deliberately thinking that it would be more
efficient when copying CDs and DVDs. But I am having a terrible time.
K3b recognizes both drives, but is unwilling to copy from one and write
to the other at the same time. It insists that it must write to the
same drive that it copies from, so it must copy to the hard drive,
eject the first media, wait for a new blank disk to be inserted, then
write to the new blank disk. If this is the best Linux software can
accomplish, then the $50 spent on the second drive was wasted.

Gnomebaker is even worse. It can see only /dev/scd0. It is unaware
that /dev/scd1 exists.

I need software that can copy from one directly to the other. Does such
exist for Linux? Or is there a way to make K3b or Gnomebaker wake up
and realize that it is possible to copy a CD or DVD without first
writing it to the hard disk?

From sbeattie at suse.de  Fri Apr 13 23:46:11 2007
From: sbeattie at suse.de (Steve Beattie)
Date: Fri, 13 Apr 2007 23:46:11 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] de-hashing entries in known_hosts file?
In-Reply-To: <462046F8.3020802@spek.org>
References: <462046F8.3020802@spek.org>
Message-ID: <20070414064611.GA29887@suse.de>

On Fri, Apr 13, 2007 at 08:14:00PM -0700, Brent Rieck wrote:
> Is it possible to reverse the hashing in the ~/.ssh/known_hosts file?

No (or at least, if implemented correctly, it shouldn't be). However,
you can use "ssh-keygen -R [hostname]" to remove a hashed host from your
known_hosts file (it keeps a backup of your known_hosts file). You can
also use "ssh-keygen -F [hostname]" to query for a given host's key.

The hashing is done to prevent the spread of worms that compromise weak
accounts and then use the known_hosts file to determine the next hosts
to attempt the weak passwords on (as the user would likely have used
the same weak password).
-- 
Steve Beattie
SUSE Labs, Novell Inc. 
<sbeattie at suse.de>
http://NxNW.org/~steve/

From bsr at spek.org  Sat Apr 14 09:53:07 2007
From: bsr at spek.org (Brent Rieck)
Date: Sat, 14 Apr 2007 09:53:07 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] de-hashing entries in known_hosts file?
In-Reply-To: <20070414064611.GA29887@suse.de>
References: <462046F8.3020802@spek.org> <20070414064611.GA29887@suse.de>
Message-ID: <462106F3.6020904@spek.org>

Steve Beattie wrote:
> On Fri, Apr 13, 2007 at 08:14:00PM -0700, Brent Rieck wrote:
>> Is it possible to reverse the hashing in the ~/.ssh/known_hosts file?
> 
> No (or at least, if implemented correctly, it shouldn't be). However,
> you can use "ssh-keygen -R [hostname]" to remove a hashed host from your
> known_hosts file (it keeps a backup of your known_hosts file). You can
> also use "ssh-keygen -F [hostname]" to query for a given host's key.

excellent, thanks.

--Brent


From nsxfreddy at gmail.com  Sat Apr 14 10:04:38 2007
From: nsxfreddy at gmail.com (Jason Martin)

Subject: [PLUG] de-hashing entries in known_hosts file?
In-Reply-To: <20070414064611.GA29887@suse.de>
References: <462046F8.3020802@spek.org> <20070414064611.GA29887@suse.de>

On 4/13/07, Steve Beattie <sbeattie at suse.de> wrote:
> On Fri, Apr 13, 2007 at 08:14:00PM -0700, Brent Rieck wrote:
> > Is it possible to reverse the hashing in the ~/.ssh/known_hosts file?
>
> No (or at least, if implemented correctly, it shouldn't be). However,
> you can use "ssh-keygen -R [hostname]" to remove a hashed host from your
> known_hosts file (it keeps a backup of your known_hosts file). You can
> also use "ssh-keygen -F [hostname]" to query for a given host's key.
>
> The hashing is done to prevent the spread of worms that compromise weak
> accounts and then use the known_hosts file to determine the next hosts
> to attempt the weak passwords on (as the user would likely have used
> the same weak password).

Interesting.  Learn something new every day.  If I was a worm, I'd aim
for your command history next.

Has this sort of worm been a problem, or is this more of a preemptive
strike?  I know there have been some brute-force ssh attacks.

From keithl at kl-ic.com  Sat Apr 14 10:35:00 2007
From: keithl at kl-ic.com (Keith Lofstrom)
Date: Sat, 14 Apr 2007 10:35:00 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Copying CD/DVD with two drives
In-Reply-To: <20070414052918.GA3205@nellump.net>

	<20070414052918.GA3205@nellump.net>
Message-ID: <20070414173500.GA17343@gate.kl-ic.com>


... John can't use both CDROM drives ...

I have two CDROM drives, and can read from one and write to the
other, both by device name ( /dev/cdrom and /dev/cdrom1 ) in
command line scripts,  and using gui apps such as K3B .

However, my drives are two different models.  The gui apps you
are using might be getting confused if both devices are exactly
the same type.  Maybe.

More likely is that both drives are not getting seen for some
reason - BIOS configuration, for example.  On the command line
in a shell window, type "dmesg | less" and see whether both
drives are seen during bootup, and what devices they are assigned
to.  There is a lot more debugging beyond that, but the dmesg
information will split the problem up.

Keith

From keithl at kl-ic.com  Sat Apr 14 10:58:52 2007
From: keithl at kl-ic.com (Keith Lofstrom)
Date: Sat, 14 Apr 2007 10:58:52 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Secret message web page
Message-ID: <20070414175852.GA17471@gate.kl-ic.com>


I set up a "secret message to Keith webpage".  I think.  As I am
not a security guru, I could use some help checking my logic.

PGP is too hard for most people to figure out.  About every 6 months
or so, some random computer-illiterate semistranger needs to send
me a short secret message, say to send a password or encryption key.
These are not high grade defense-level secrets, but a little more 
than I want to trust to clear-text email.

Hence the webpage.  It is a simple form, accessable through port 443
(SSL https web service) on my server:
--------------------------------------------------------------------
<!DOCTYPE HTML PUBLIC "-//W3C//DTD HTML 4.0 Transitional//EN"><html><head>
<meta name="description" content="Send Keith a Message form">
<title>Send Keith a Message</title>
</head><body>
<FORM METHOD=POST ACTION="https://www.keithl.com/cgibin/XXXXXXXXX.cgi">
   <INPUT TYPE="hidden" NAME="FORMVERSION" VALUE="XXXXXXXX.html 2007Apr14 KHL">
   Enter text to send to Keith Lofstrom in this text box
   <INPUT TYPE="submit" NAME="EXECUTE_OPTION" VALUE="Send page to Keith">
   <br><TEXTAREA ROWS=20 COLS=90 NAME="TEXT53"></TEXTAREA>
</FORM></body></html>
--------------------------------------------------------------------

The POST data is sent to the XXXXXXXX.cgi script (name obscured) on
my server, which sends back a confirmation page (https of course)
and emails me (internally, over a VPN link) the information and 
some environment information such as the date, the script and form
names, the IP address of the remote browser, and whether HTTPS is
turned on.

This is not bulletproof;  it is a self-signed cert, for example, so
it is somewhat vulnerable to a man-in-the-middle attack.  But hey,
if Eve works that hard to access the information, she would be more
interesting to talk to, anyway.  :-)

Is this an acceptable approach, or it it too weak to bother with?

Keith
