> K3b, GnomeBaker and Brasero.


I'm not sure what you want to do is advisable, honestly.  Most CD-ROM
copying apps default to copying files to a temporary directory on the
hard drive first because it's much safer.  Copying directly from CD-ROM
to CD-ROM introduces all sorts of possibilities for errors -- jitter
errors, uneven read/write speeds, I/O errors, etc.  All of the advice I
ever saw about copying CD-ROMs on usenet, in a Yahoo group I used to
belong to devoted to CDR issues, recommended not trying to make a direct
CD-to-CDR copy.  Your chances of ending up with coasters are much
greater.

See [1] here what the Feurio FAQ has to say about it.  Quote: "So
'on-the-Fly' copy should only be used if e.g. there is no room on the
harddisk for an image. Making a copy via an image file is always much,
much safer!"

[1] http://www.feurio.com/English/faq/faq_vocable_ontheflycopy.shtml

> --------------------------------------------------------------------
> <!DOCTYPE HTML PUBLIC "-//W3C//DTD HTML 4.0 Transitional//EN"><html><head>
> <meta name="description" content="Send Keith a Message form">

> </head><body>
> <FORM METHOD=POST ACTION="https://www.keithl.com/cgibin/XXXXXXXXX.cgi">

> --------------------------------------------------------------------

> and emails me (internally, over a VPN link) the information and
> some environment information such as the date, the script and form
> names, the IP address of the remote browser, and whether HTTPS is
> turned on.
>

> it is somewhat vulnerable to a man-in-the-middle attack.  But hey,
> if Eve works that hard to access the information, she would be more
> interesting to talk to, anyway.  :-)
>

This provides you with no real security, since a MITM attacker can
intercept the initial connection and provide their own self-signed
certificate to the browser, simultaneously establishing a connection
to your server.  They can then decrypt and re-encrypt the session.
Since your cert is self-signed, the user has no way to determine
whether your cert or the MITM cert is legit.

If you can provide the cert signature to the person ahead of time,
they could verify it when the self-signed warning pops up.

Also, I assume you have considered the fact that *you* have no way of
verifying the identity of the sender?  If the interface is open it
could be subject to spam.

Jason

From scratchcomputing at gmail.com  Sat Apr 14 14:39:18 2007
From: scratchcomputing at gmail.com (Eric Wilhelm)
Date: Sat, 14 Apr 2007 14:39:18 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Secret message web page
In-Reply-To: <c29537840704141216q3f83ec03w5937d7ae1a6b7548@mail.gmail.com>

Message-ID: <200704141439.18308.ewilhelm@cpan.org>

I'll second the issues of the self-signed cert.  You really do need the 
"I am who I say I am" bit for this whole thing to work.  (Whatever 
happened to the freegeek semi-monthly keysigning anyway?)

# from Jason Martin
# on Saturday 14 April 2007 12:16 pm:

>If the interface is open it could be subject to spam.

My thoughts exactly.  Anything resembling a POSTable form anywhere on 
your website will be immediately pelted by bots (as soon as they find 
it) looking for naive e-mail scripts that don't protect the header from 
being appended by the form content.  If you're not careful, the page 
could even be sending spam to *elsewhere* without you knowing it 
(unless you're watching the server log.)

You want to be sure you don't have *that* sort of cgi script.

>Also, I assume you have considered the fact that *you* have no way of
>verifying the identity of the sender? 

You probably also want to make it a password-protected page.  Hand-out a 
one-time password to your visitor over the phone (or e-mail if you 
must, since the risk is greatly reduced by the expiry window.)

>On 4/14/07, Keith Lofstrom <keithl at kl-ic.com> wrote:
>> ... cgibin/XXXXXXXXX.cgi

If you *really* wanted it tested, you would post the real url here where 
the scambots could find it :-D

I installed CentOS 5 amd64 on my new desktop this morning and spent
some time playing with it. Pretty cool. It found and autoconfigured
everything. I like it a lot.

There was one problem with the installation. When anaconda got to the
partitioning part I told it to create a RAID 1 partition of 20 GB to
install itself on (md2). My intention was not to disturb the Fedora 7
installation on md1. It created the partition without incident, but as
I finished the partitioning it insisted that it had to install Grub on
md2. The only other option was not to install a boot loader. I knew
what would happen if I continued and let it install Grub on md2, but I
continued anyway and, sure enough, it set up the new Grub as the one to
boot to. I think I should have told it not to install a boot loader and
then add it to the boot loader on md1. Oh well. I can fix that later.
My complaint is twofold -- 1) anaconda should have detected the
installation of Fedora 7 on md1, realized it probably had a boot
loader, and offered to add CentOS to that menu.lst and, 2) the option
not to install a boot loader should have some text with it to describe
why one might choose this option. 

So now it is running and I have it configured and stuff. It uses
2.6.18-8, which will cause the gigabit Realtek on the motherboard to
cause a kernel panic if I don't leave the Realtek disabled in the BIOS.
Fedora 7 test2 updated itself the other day to 2.6.20-something. And I
also note that CentOS 5 comes with OpenOffice.org 2.0.4, where Fedora 7
and Feisty come with 2.2. 

It also failed to mount the Fedora 7 partition, but I was able to do so
manually. However, I added a line to /etc/fstab to make it mount md1 on
boot, but the line doesn't work. 

In the process of playing with it I managed to upgooberize yum. It
won't launch. From the command line I get:

There was a problem importing one of the python modules required to run
yum. The error leading to this problem was: 
No module named cElementTree
Please install a package which provides this module or verify that the
module is installed properly. It's possible that the above module
doesn't match the current version of Python, which is: 
2.4.3 (#1 March 14, 2007, 19:01:42) 
(GCC 4.1.1 20070105 (Red hat 4.1.1-52))

Well, gosh. I'd really like to install the module that yum needs except
that it seems that yum doesn't work so I can't install anything. Does
this thing come with a backup-yum or something? And does anyone have
any idea what I need to install to get yum working again? Assuming I
can figure out how to install things, should I just reinstall yum?

From nsxfreddy at gmail.com  Sun Apr 15 19:43:26 2007

Subject: [PLUG] Secret message web page
In-Reply-To: <c29537840704141216q3f83ec03w5937d7ae1a6b7548@mail.gmail.com>

On 4/14/07, Jason Martin <nsxfreddy at gmail.com> wrote:
> On 4/14/07, Keith Lofstrom <keithl at kl-ic.com> wrote:
> >
> > I set up a "secret message to Keith webpage".  I think.  As I am
> > not a security guru, I could use some help checking my logic.
> >
> > PGP is too hard for most people to figure out.  About every 6 months
> > or so, some random computer-illiterate semistranger needs to send
> > me a short secret message, say to send a password or encryption key.
> > These are not high grade defense-level secrets, but a little more
> > than I want to trust to clear-text email.
> >
> > Hence the webpage.  It is a simple form, accessable through port 443
> > (SSL https web service) on my server:
> > --------------------------------------------------------------------
> > <!DOCTYPE HTML PUBLIC "-//W3C//DTD HTML 4.0 Transitional//EN"><html><head>
> > <meta name="description" content="Send Keith a Message form">
> > <title>Send Keith a Message</title>
> > </head><body>
> > <FORM METHOD=POST ACTION="https://www.keithl.com/cgibin/XXXXXXXXX.cgi">
> >    <INPUT TYPE="hidden" NAME="FORMVERSION" VALUE="XXXXXXXX.html 2007Apr14 KHL">
> >    Enter text to send to Keith Lofstrom in this text box
> >    <INPUT TYPE="submit" NAME="EXECUTE_OPTION" VALUE="Send page to Keith">
> >    <br><TEXTAREA ROWS=20 COLS=90 NAME="TEXT53"></TEXTAREA>
> > </FORM></body></html>
> > --------------------------------------------------------------------
> >
> > The POST data is sent to the XXXXXXXX.cgi script (name obscured) on
> > my server, which sends back a confirmation page (https of course)
> > and emails me (internally, over a VPN link) the information and
> > some environment information such as the date, the script and form
> > names, the IP address of the remote browser, and whether HTTPS is
> > turned on.
> >
> > This is not bulletproof;  it is a self-signed cert, for example, so
> > it is somewhat vulnerable to a man-in-the-middle attack.  But hey,
> > if Eve works that hard to access the information, she would be more
> > interesting to talk to, anyway.  :-)
> >
> > Is this an acceptable approach, or it it too weak to bother with?
>
> This provides you with no real security, since a MITM attacker can
> intercept the initial connection and provide their own self-signed
> certificate to the browser, simultaneously establishing a connection
> to your server.  They can then decrypt and re-encrypt the session.
> Since your cert is self-signed, the user has no way to determine
> whether your cert or the MITM cert is legit.
>
> If you can provide the cert signature to the person ahead of time,
> they could verify it when the self-signed warning pops up.

It occurred to me that I focused on the negatives of your approach,
and missed one positive.  With the self-signed certificate, you do
ensure that the message cannot be read by a *passive* attacker (i.e.
someone just sniffing and capturing the traffic).  In order to read it
the attacker must actively perform a MITM attack.  So you are in a
sense providing a bit more security.

Jason


From charlie at schluting.com  Mon Apr 16 09:02:50 2007
From: charlie at schluting.com (Charlie Schluting)
Date: Mon, 16 Apr 2007 09:02:50 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Secret message web page
In-Reply-To: <c29537840704151943j31488d53n4dde2d903b830db0@mail.gmail.com>
References: <20070414175852.GA17471@gate.kl-ic.com>	<c29537840704141216q3f83ec03w5937d7ae1a6b7548@mail.gmail.com>
	<c29537840704151943j31488d53n4dde2d903b830db0@mail.gmail.com>
Message-ID: <46239E2A.90102@schluting.com>

On 4/15/07 7:43 PM, Jason Martin wrote:
> 
> It occurred to me that I focused on the negatives of your approach,
> and missed one positive.  With the self-signed certificate, you do
> ensure that the message cannot be read by a *passive* attacker (i.e.
> someone just sniffing and capturing the traffic).  In order to read it
> the attacker must actively perform a MITM attack.  So you are in a
> sense providing a bit more security.


Everyone is always uppidy about MITM attacks. The truth is, nobody can
MITM you out on the big bad internet. That'd require compromising an
ISP's core router, and mirroring a port to a compromised box. Oh, and
the ability to inject packets, so said compromised box would have to be
attached to the same router. It's very unlikely to find such an environment.

Just like "IP addresses are easy to spoof." They aren't, once you cross
out onto the Internet. I guarantee (almost) that your ISP won't send
packets that aren't sourced from their address space. And your ISP's ISP
will most certainly drop packets that aren't sourced appropriately. (See
BCP38 - yes, people do it)

So MITM attacks will happen on your local internal network, or on the
other person's. The local subnet is always the most insecure. Now, if
your remote friend does get compromised, and an attacker does happen to
be camped on their router (not likely), they'd never know it. They'd be
used to arriving at your form and getting a cert error, so they'd just
continue as normal. You could have them "install" the cert and tell
their browser to always accept it, but then you get back to that ease of
use issue.

If this will be used by many people, I'd suggest password protecting it
first of all. Buying a $20 SSL cert from godaddy is also a good idea.
(Yes, their CA cert is in everyone's browser)

 -Charlie



From ed at alcpress.com  Mon Apr 16 09:29:36 2007
From: ed at alcpress.com (Ed Sawicki)
Date: Mon, 16 Apr 2007 09:29:36 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Secret message web page
In-Reply-To: <20070414175852.GA17471@gate.kl-ic.com>
References: <20070414175852.GA17471@gate.kl-ic.com>
Message-ID: <4623A470.50502@alcpress.com>

Keith Lofstrom wrote:
> I set up a "secret message to Keith webpage".  I think.  As I am
> not a security guru, I could use some help checking my logic.
> 
> PGP is too hard for most people to figure out.  About every 6 months
> or so, some random computer-illiterate semistranger needs to send
> me a short secret message, say to send a password or encryption key.
> These are not high grade defense-level secrets, but a little more 
> than I want to trust to clear-text email.
> 
> Hence the webpage.  It is a simple form, accessable through port 443
> (SSL https web service) on my server:
> --------------------------------------------------------------------
> <!DOCTYPE HTML PUBLIC "-//W3C//DTD HTML 4.0 Transitional//EN"><html><head>
> <meta name="description" content="Send Keith a Message form">
> <title>Send Keith a Message</title>
> </head><body>
> <FORM METHOD=POST ACTION="https://www.keithl.com/cgibin/XXXXXXXXX.cgi">
>    <INPUT TYPE="hidden" NAME="FORMVERSION" VALUE="XXXXXXXX.html 2007Apr14 KHL">
>    Enter text to send to Keith Lofstrom in this text box
>    <INPUT TYPE="submit" NAME="EXECUTE_OPTION" VALUE="Send page to Keith">
>    <br><TEXTAREA ROWS=20 COLS=90 NAME="TEXT53"></TEXTAREA>
> </FORM></body></html>
> --------------------------------------------------------------------
> 
> The POST data is sent to the XXXXXXXX.cgi script (name obscured) on
> my server, which sends back a confirmation page (https of course)
> and emails me (internally, over a VPN link) the information and 
> some environment information such as the date, the script and form
> names, the IP address of the remote browser, and whether HTTPS is
> turned on.
> 
> This is not bulletproof;  it is a self-signed cert, for example, so
> it is somewhat vulnerable to a man-in-the-middle attack.  But hey,
> if Eve works that hard to access the information, she would be more
> interesting to talk to, anyway.  :-)
> 
> Is this an acceptable approach, or it it too weak to bother with?

Self-signed certificates are not necessarily more vulnerable
to MITM attacks than certificates signed by the Certificate
Authorities that Web browsers know about. Just get these
people to import your CA certificate into their browsers.
Of course, the problem here is how do they know if they're
importing a legitimate certificate for your CA. You can
provide this assurance to them by having your Web site
protected by a certificate issued by a CA that is already
known to their browser.

You can further reduce the risk of MITM (and other attacks)
by having them use browser-side certificates (so your Web
server knows the client is genuine). You can issue these
certificates to them using your own CA.

Then you have the problem of ensuring your CGI script is secure.

I favor not using browsers and Web servers when you have
to move sensitive data. Take it "out of band". Provide them
a program that knows how to send the data to you over a
secure, authenticated channel that's independent of the rest
of the world. If they're running Windows, you're a bit
limited but there's Putty that can be wrapped up in a bit of
Python, tclkit, Java WebStart, etc.

Ed




> Keith
>

From garton.tim at gmail.com  Mon Apr 16 09:42:37 2007
From: garton.tim at gmail.com (Tim Garton)
Date: Mon, 16 Apr 2007 09:42:37 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] CentOS 5 is cool, but ...
In-Reply-To: <20070414195307.194a3706.johnxj@comcast.net>
References: <20070414195307.194a3706.johnxj@comcast.net>
Message-ID: <8fcf3b560704160942m4ee3e412n9c2ed2190ca5de35@mail.gmail.com>

I guess you could try installing the rpm directly that contains that
module.  I believe it would be the "python-elementtree" rpm:
http://ftp.osuosl.org/pub/centos/5.0/os/x86_64/CentOS/python-elementtree-1.2.6-5.x86_64.rpm

As for the detection and updating of grub for other distros (in your case
FC7), I don't think it's a very wise idea for one distro to try to update
another distro's grub configuration.  Some distros may do some pretty funky
stuff and if CentOS were to go an try to update them, it may just end up
hosing the configuration completely.  A better solution is to offer to
install it's own version of grub and add entries for the other OS, or leave
everything alone and make the user set things up.  Which is sort of what it
sounds like CentOS did, although I agree that some descriptive text
describing these options would be nice.

Tim

Message-ID: <c29537840704161028l32b5c117w6a9d0c8fc0fc4658@mail.gmail.com>

On 4/16/07, Wil Cooley <wcooley at nakedape.cc> wrote:
> With certs available so cheaply, it's probably worthwhile if for no
> other reason than to not have to repeatedly explain to people that they
> just need to accept the cert and move on.

Not to mention it probably isn't a good idea to train your
non-security-aware acquaintances to automatically accept those warning
dialogs.

From nsxfreddy at gmail.com  Mon Apr 16 10:30:55 2007
From: nsxfreddy at gmail.com (Jason Martin)
Date: Mon, 16 Apr 2007 10:30:55 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Secret message web page
In-Reply-To: <46239E2A.90102@schluting.com>
References: <20070414175852.GA17471@gate.kl-ic.com>
	<c29537840704141216q3f83ec03w5937d7ae1a6b7548@mail.gmail.com>
	<c29537840704151943j31488d53n4dde2d903b830db0@mail.gmail.com>
	<46239E2A.90102@schluting.com>
Message-ID: <c29537840704161030x3a42ab3fk4db10a594d20edd0@mail.gmail.com>

On 4/16/07, Charlie Schluting <charlie at schluting.com> wrote:
> On 4/15/07 7:43 PM, Jason Martin wrote:
> >
> > It occurred to me that I focused on the negatives of your approach,
> > and missed one positive.  With the self-signed certificate, you do
> > ensure that the message cannot be read by a *passive* attacker (i.e.
> > someone just sniffing and capturing the traffic).  In order to read it
> > the attacker must actively perform a MITM attack.  So you are in a
> > sense providing a bit more security.
>
> Everyone is always uppidy about MITM attacks. The truth is, nobody can
> MITM you out on the big bad internet. That'd require compromising an
> ISP's core router, and mirroring a port to a compromised box. Oh, and
> the ability to inject packets, so said compromised box would have to be
> attached to the same router. It's very unlikely to find such an environment.

You're right, it is much more likely to have a rogue administrator in
that ISP.  Or a rogue government placing illegal wiretaps everywhere.

I agree with you, that it is unlikely someone with MITM Keith's
connections, which is why I sent my second message.  But he should be
aware of what could happen, if someone wanted that information bad
enough.

> Just like "IP addresses are easy to spoof." They aren't, once you cross
> out onto the Internet. I guarantee (almost) that your ISP won't send
> packets that aren't sourced from their address space. And your ISP's ISP
> will most certainly drop packets that aren't sourced appropriately. (See
> BCP38 - yes, people do it)

That is true... now.  If that had always been true, then smurf attacks
never would have been an issue.

> So MITM attacks will happen on your local internal network, or on the
> other person's. The local subnet is always the most insecure. Now, if
> your remote friend does get compromised, and an attacker does happen to
> be camped on their router (not likely), they'd never know it. They'd be
> used to arriving at your form and getting a cert error, so they'd just
> continue as normal. You could have them "install" the cert and tell
> their browser to always accept it, but then you get back to that ease of
> use issue.
>
> If this will be used by many people, I'd suggest password protecting it
> first of all. Buying a $20 SSL cert from godaddy is also a good idea.
> (Yes, their CA cert is in everyone's browser)

And there's the cure-all (assuming Keith has a dedicated IP for his
server).  I'm not sure I'd go with godaddy, since they seem to be a
bit spineless on their customer's legal rights
(http://seclists.org/nmap-hackers/2007/0000.html), but in general
getting a cert is not that expensive and would solve this issue.

Jason


From qhartman at gmail.com  Mon Apr 16 10:42:06 2007
From: qhartman at gmail.com (Quentin Hartman)
Date: Mon, 16 Apr 2007 10:42:06 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Offsite Fileserver Replication/Mirroring
In-Reply-To: <8fcf3b560704161021u62b4423am2e4b5fe76154000a@mail.gmail.com>
References: <8fcf3b560704161021u62b4423am2e4b5fe76154000a@mail.gmail.com>
Message-ID: <8975a95f0704161042g5c33ef9va848ea91d9d3939b@mail.gmail.com>

On 4/16/07, Tim Garton <garton.tim at gmail.com> wrote:
>
> All,
>     We have a Samba FS with about 500GB worth of data and are looking to
> develop a plan for disaster recovery that would allow for as little
> downtime
> as possible.  First off I was wondering if people had suggestions on
> measuring the amount of change to the FS on a daily/weekly basis so I can
> get an idea of space and bandwidth needs.


I don't know of any "easy" semi-automated way to do this. One way to do this
would be to run rdiff-backup to another on-site machine. If you examine the
size of the diff after your second backup, it should show you the volume of
the data change. Repeat that a couple times and you can get an idea of the
rate of change.


  Secondly, beyond the more
> traditional approach of doing backups to tape/disk, I was wondering if
> people had thoughts/experience with doing some kind of live offsite
> mirror/replica of the FS.  This has the advantage of if the main FS/site
> goes down, people could just VPN to the offsite location and continue
> working as normal, albiet somewhat more slowly.  Is it feasible?  (The FS
> is
> connected via Comcast Business Cable - 8MB down/1MB up)  I guess this will
> depend on how much change takes place.  Any recommended tools to do this?
> Obviously regular rsync's would do it, but a quick google search turns up
> interesting results like tsyncd, csync2, drbd, openafs.  Any thoughts or
> advice on the topic are appreciated.



I've only seen this done in practice on Windows with XOSoft WanSync (now
owned by CA), which is very spendy ($2200+ per machine, so $4500+ minimum to
get started). According to the guy I spoke with who actually ran some
machines that used it, it worked well and for his business it was money well
spent. I'd love to hear what you manage to come up with for Linux machines.
I'm working on getting our backup regimen re-done right now as well and this
sort of solution is interesting to me.

-- 
-Regards-

-Quentin Hartman-


From qhartman at gmail.com  Mon Apr 16 10:58:57 2007

	<65282.170.135.112.12.1176741061.squirrel@mail.patch.com>
Message-ID: <8975a95f0704161058l38cf2280mcf6b02c24d23d2fe@mail.gmail.com>

On 4/16/07, Michael Rasmussen <mikeraz at patch.com> wrote:
>
>
> Charlie Schluting wrote:
> > Everyone is always uppidy about MITM attacks. The truth is...
>
> The truth is Keith's use for this isn't worth the effort of setting up a
> MITM.
>
> He has described a system that makes the cost of compromise higher than
> the reward
> of compromising.  That's good security.


I agree with this sentiment entirely. However, looking at the problem from
this angle, is the information sensitive enough to warrant the amount of
work required to intercept the plaintext email that would have been sent
normally? Only Keith can really answer that, but as mentioned previously
with regards to MITM attacks, I'd wager that the effort required to
intercept that mail is pretty substantial already, corrupt ISP admins aside.

Other than that, this approach has a couple of other effects. It heightens
awareness of security amongst people who wouldn't really think about it
otherwise (good), but it does so in a non-standard way (bad) that isn't
likely to be usable when working with someone other than Keith (bad) but it
is something that they can grasp (good).

Personally, my solution to this problem is to either get this sort of
information over the phone or in person when possible. When that is not
possible I change the password immediately if it allows access to sensitive
information. For most stuff though, the value of my secrets are so low that
anyone going to the trouble of intercepting them is in for a net loss on the
project, so I don't worry about it much.

-- 
-Regards-

-Quentin Hartman-

All,
    We have a Samba FS with about 500GB worth of data and are looking to
develop a plan for disaster recovery that would allow for as little downtime
as possible.  First off I was wondering if people had suggestions on
measuring the amount of change to the FS on a daily/weekly basis so I can
get an idea of space and bandwidth needs.  Secondly, beyond the more
traditional approach of doing backups to tape/disk, I was wondering if
people had thoughts/experience with doing some kind of live offsite
mirror/replica of the FS.  This has the advantage of if the main FS/site
goes down, people could just VPN to the offsite location and continue
working as normal, albiet somewhat more slowly.  Is it feasible?  (The FS is
connected via Comcast Business Cable - 8MB down/1MB up)  I guess this will
depend on how much change takes place.  Any recommended tools to do this?
Obviously regular rsync's would do it, but a quick google search turns up
interesting results like tsyncd, csync2, drbd, openafs.  Any thoughts or
advice on the topic are appreciated.

Tim

From wcooley at nakedape.cc  Mon Apr 16 11:34:24 2007
From: wcooley at nakedape.cc (Wil Cooley)
Date: Mon, 16 Apr 2007 11:34:24 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Godaddy Certs - was Secret message web page
In-Reply-To: <20070416180334.GA19669@gate.kl-ic.com>
References: <20070414175852.GA17471@gate.kl-ic.com>	<c29537840704141216q3f83ec03w5937d7ae1a6b7548@mail.gmail.com>	<c29537840704151943j31488d53n4dde2d903b830db0@mail.gmail.com>	<46239E2A.90102@schluting.com>
	<20070416180334.GA19669@gate.kl-ic.com>
Message-ID: <4623C1B0.6060101@nakedape.cc>

Keith Lofstrom wrote:
> On Mon, Apr 16, 2007 at 09:02:50AM -0700, Charlie Schluting wrote:
>> If this will be used by many people, I'd suggest password protecting it
>> first of all. Buying a $20 SSL cert from godaddy is also a good idea.
>> (Yes, their CA cert is in everyone's browser)
> 
> That is an excellent suggestion.  While this page won't be used by
> many people, I may set up something that is, and those little popup
> warnings are annoying.  In the past, certs usually cost hundreds of
> dollars a year, and I put up with a little annoyance to avoid that
> cost.  But $20/year is quite affordable.
> 
> I use dyndns for all my other registration and DNS needs.  They don't
> offer certs yet, but perhaps they will in the future, so I will check
> with them first.  I don't know how long it takes for a CA cert to 
> become available to people's browsers, or what the process involves,
> but if this happens quickly I will try to go with one provider.

You have to generate a private key and a certificate-signing request 
(CSR), then send or upload the CSR to the provider.  The provider 
generates a certificate which you then download and point your web 
server at.  Sometimes there is an intermediate certificate that you need 
to install; basically it is another certificate from your provider 
signed by a bigger provider, because the actual provider's cert wasn't 
in the standard keystore.  I've had to do this with one of the cheap 
cert providers; it's generally one additional line of configuration and 
the end users do not notice.

Another benefit, of course, is that if you have other services on the 
host that you want protected you can use it for them also--just as long 
as the hostname in use matches.  If you had a server called 
'home.kl-ic.com' that you were going to host this web app and also 
provided SMTPS and IMAPS so you could access your e-mail remotely, you 
can use this same cert with all the servers.

You might find my handful of notes useful: 
http://nakedape.cc/wiki/ApplicationNotes_2fSslNotes

Wil

>> > You have to generate a private key and a certificate-signing request
>> (CSR),
>> > then send or upload the CSR to the provider.  The provider generates a
>> > certificate which you then download and point your web server
>> at.  Sometimes
>> > there is an intermediate certificate that you need to install; basically
>> it
>> > is another certificate from your provider signed by a bigger provider,
>> > because the actual provider's cert wasn't in the standard
>> keystore.  I've had
>> > to do this with one of the cheap cert providers; it's generally one
>> > additional line of configuration and the end users do not notice.
>> >
>> > Another benefit, of course, is that if you have other services on the
>> host
>> > that you want protected you can use it for them also--just as long as
>> the
>> > hostname in use matches.  If you had a server called 'home.kl-ic.com'
>> that
>> > you were going to host this web app and also provided SMTPS and IMAPS so
>> you
>> > could access your e-mail remotely, you can use this same cert with all
>> the
>> > servers.
>> >
>> > You might find my handful of notes useful:
>> > http://nakedape.cc/wiki/ApplicationNotes_2fSslNotes
>>

>> sources, so I won't use them.
>>

From carla at bratgrrl.com  Mon Apr 16 17:21:57 2007
From: carla at bratgrrl.com (Carla Schroder)
Date: Mon, 16 Apr 2007 17:21:57 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] when to blackhole routes?
Message-ID: <200704161721.57675.carla@bratgrrl.com>

Under what circumstances does it make sense to blackhole routes? Assume you 
are using the null0 interface in Quagga. An obvious example is blocking 
spammers or other pests, like out-of-control web spiders. What about blocking 
RFC 1918 addresses entering or leaving your network with routing commands 
instead of iptables rules? Isn't that more efficient?

Isn't blackholing an invitation to a syn flood attack anyway? Can you use the 
ip command's 'unreachable' rule option, which sends a 'Network is 
unreachable' error?

From johnxj at comcast.net  Tue Apr 17 00:09:27 2007
From: johnxj at comcast.net (John Jason Jordan)
Date: Tue, 17 Apr 2007 00:09:27 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] - CentOS 5, + Debian Etch
Message-ID: <20070417000927.3e7a7f01.johnxj@comcast.net>

This evening I decided to wipe out CentOS 5 and try Debian Etch amd64
(final) on my new computer. Installation went without issue, although
the installer is not graphical. The installer worked, albeit parts were
a bit confusing. 

Before installing it I removed the Intel 10/100 PCI card that I got
from Free Geek, then went into the BIOS and enabled the onboard Realtek
8169 gigabit. With other distros the Realtek was either ignored or it
would cause a kernel panic, so I had left it disabled. The first bit of
good news is that Debian Etch is the first distro that not only found
it but autconfigured it without so much as a hiccup. Yay!

The bad news is that it failed to find the onboard sound. This is the
first distro that failed to find the sound chip. However, I have a
guess -- the sound is also Realtek, and the sound and the gigabit are
the only Realtek items. 

More bad news is that it failed to detect the nVidia GeForce 6100 and
the Viewsonics G90f monitor. Fedora 7 and CentOS both found them and
autoconfigured them. However, Feisty Beta also failed to find them.
Maybe it's a Debian v. RPM thing.

I also had to add "noapic acpi=off" to keep the mouse click from dying
and to make the clock correctly. But I've had to do that with every
distro so far.

It installed IceWeasel instead of straight Firefox, and the default
browser is Epiphany. Java works, but no flash out of the box.
OpenOffice.org is only 2.02, but everything works. I put a movie in the
drive and Totem popped up and played it fine (but sans sound).

Now off to fix stuff and play with it.

> the only Realtek items. 

The Debian User list is pretty helpful with issues like that.  The
subscription page for all official Debian lists (there are lots of
them!) is here:

http://www.debian.org/MailingLists/subscribe

You probably want the plain ol' 'debian-user,' unless you want to test
out your Catalan, Icelandic, or Ukranian language skills. :-)

> 
> More bad news is that it failed to detect the nVidia GeForce 6100 and
> the Viewsonics G90f monitor. Fedora 7 and CentOS both found them and
> autoconfigured them. However, Feisty Beta also failed to find them.
> Maybe it's a Debian v. RPM thing.

Ditto above.

> 
> I also had to add "noapic acpi=off" to keep the mouse click from dying
> and to make the clock correctly. But I've had to do that with every
> distro so far.
> 
> It installed IceWeasel instead of straight Firefox, and the default
> browser is Epiphany. Java works, but no flash out of the box.
> OpenOffice.org is only 2.02, but everything works. I put a movie in the
> drive and Totem popped up and played it fine (but sans sound).

The officially unofficial repository for all evil proprietary stuff is
here:

http://www.debian-multimedia.org/

If you're sticking with Etch, you'll want to add the following to
your /etc/apt/sources.list:

deb http://www.debian-multimedia.org etch main

That will get you access to flash, the packages you need to play back
commercial DVDs, w32codecs, and other stuff.

But maybe you knew that already.

IMO, Debian testing is the sweet spot for desktop usage.  The new
testing repository is "Lenny."  I have been using Etch for a least the
past year, while it was testing, and experienced almost no problems.
It's more stable than many distro's releases, tends to have fewer bugs
and fewer uninstallable packages than Ubuntu's releases.  At the same
time, it generally stays pretty current, often bordering on
bleeding-edge.  Big packages, like new versions of DEs, will take a few
months to migrate to testing from Sid (unstable), but most packages move
from Sid to testing in a matter of days.  The gutsy go ahead and run
Sid.  Sid, however, is almost guaranteed to have some breakage once in a
while, so you have to be prepared to deal with that.  From my POV, Sid
usually acts as a nice buffer for catching and solving the bigger
problems before they hit testing.  That's not to say testing is always
bug free, but they tend to be niggly rather than show-stopping bugs.

But I wouldn't upgrade to testing just yet.  There will probably be a
period of instabilty as packages that have been held back (while Debian
was focussed on releasing Etch) flood into unstable and then into
testing.  I figure on waiting a month or two and monitoring the mailing
lists to find the appropriate time to make the jump.

But it's not critical. Synaptic lists 17,504 packages, so I probably

> IMO, Debian testing is the sweet spot for desktop usage. 

After having fiddled over the past couple of weeks with various
distros, including several RPM based distros, I think I have to agree
in general about Debian. Nothing in the RPM world comes close to the
ease of package management on Debian. I take back all the kvetches I
voiced in the past about Synaptic. However, I think I'll stick with
Etch for now. The purpose of the new computer is 1) to give me an
alternate way to get on the net when I hose my laptop again (a regular
occurrence) and, 2) to function as a network storage place for backups.

I'm not ready to commit fully to straight Debian Etch for the new
computer. I plan to try out Feisty as soon as it is final, then I'll
settle on something. Maybe I'll just use both as a dual boot.

Speaking of backups, my laptop has not been seriously backed up for
some time. All I have is temp backups to my thumb drive. So this
morning I got NFS working so the new computer can see the laptop.
(Thanks to William at the Clinic for teaching me how to get that
going). However, I still need to create the partition for the backups
on the new computer. There are 232 GB free on each of the two drives
and I want to create a RAID 1 partition. I opened gparted in Etch, but
it does not seem to be able to create a RAID. It can see existing RAID
partitions, and it can create new regular partitions, but there is no
way to tell it to create the new partition for a RAID. I searched in
Synaptic on "raid" but nothing showed up that said it could create a
RAID partition. I could boot to the install DVD and use its partition
tool, but is there some other tool in Etch that will create a RAID
partition on unused space?

There are some around, but I'm not sure if any of them are sponsored by
Debian.  I'm not partial to web forums myself, I much prefer mailing
lists and usenet.  (Note that all the Debian mailing lists can be
accessed via gmane if you prefer nntp to email; also, I think you can
get to them via Google Groups.)  Debian is definitely mailing-list
centric.

Here are some Debian-specific rss feeds I'm subbed to, that I find
helpful:

http://www.debianadmin.com/
http://debaday.debian.net/
http://www.debian-administration.org/
http://planet.debian.org/

Another good feed, not Debian-specific, but often has excellent Debian
(and Ubuntu) and general Linux content:

http://linuxhelp.blogspot.com/index.html

And some helpful websites:

http://www.debianhelp.org/
http://www.debianhelp.co.uk/index.php
http://newbiedoc.berlios.de/wiki/Main_Page

And the official wiki:
http://wiki.debian.org/FrontPage

I've only ever edited my /etc/apt/sources.list by hand.  I'm not sure
why the apt-key failed.  There used to be explicit instructions on how
to install the GPG key on the debian-multimedia website (in the [1]
FAQ), but now all it says is:

   apt GPG key : 
   Install the debian-multimedia-keyring package.

So maybe that's all you need to do.

I'm a very big fan of aptitude (vs. synaptic or apt-get & related).  It
does everything, is copiously documented, and works with or without X
running.  Maybe I'm overly cautious, but I prefer doing the
update/upgrade thing without X running.  Sometimes there will be various
library upgrades that will end up requiring a restart of X anyway or
bork things up if you don't, so I just got in the habit of doing it from
a virtual terminal.  Aptitude's ncurses interface is as easy to use as a
GUI interface once you play with it a bit.

Yeah, I've come to the conclusion that for me it's worth setting aside
some gigabytes for something I *know* will work.  Gives me peace of
mind.  If all else fails, I can always boot into Etch.  And another
route to check out, as an alternative to upgrading to testing, is
backports:

http://www.backports.org/dokuwiki/doku.php

It's pretty useless right now because Etch was just released, but it'll
get back up to speed soon enough.  It's a painless way to get new
versions of popular packages into Etch.

 Let us know how it stacks up you've become PLUG's own distro watch and I
mean that in a good way :) I haven't used Ubuntu in nearly 2 years and it
wasn't really Ubuntu
http://www.theregister.co.uk/2006/01/31/google_goes_desktop_linux/ it was a
custom version we used at Google.

My gripes at the time were that the installer absolutely sucked, I'm used to
bare bones ncurses installers ala Slackware but this thing made Slackware's
look like it had just been on an episode of "Pimp my installer". My other
beef was lack of package selection during install .. you got what they gave
you then had to go from there ... I'm a type A personality .. I don't want
to have to spend hours/days/weeks removing packages along with their
dependencies that I didn't want installed in the first place.

I admit I haven't tried Debian in something like 5 or 6 years because when I
attempted an install the media would boot and hang at a black screen on my
hardware and I was just too lazy to figure out what the problem was :) But I
am proud to say I have downloaded Etch and am going to give it a spin along
with something called KateOS which is basically Slackware with a graphical
installer and an up to date kernel ;-)

Cheers,
Drew-

From keithl at kl-ic.com  Thu Apr 19 07:56:09 2007
From: keithl at kl-ic.com (Keith Lofstrom)
Date: Thu, 19 Apr 2007 07:56:09 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] incoming fax receiving services
Message-ID: <20070419145609.GB30610@gate.kl-ic.com>


For the last 15 years, I have maintained an incoming fax/modem line.
I get only about 5 legitimate faxes a year now (almost none are local),
and about 10 spam faxes a week.  I no longer use the modem for anything.  
Time to ditch the fax line and save some money.

There are online services that will receive faxes;  some are $10/mo,
some are free.  They email TIFFs or sometimes a special format.  They
usually receive on a dedicated number.  Any suggestions for a Linux
friendly, open format service?

Also, I don't think the senders would object to dialing into an
extension, if the sending fax machine permits it.  Is this feasable?
An extension would weed out most of the spam faxes, and might save
money for the receiving service.  Ideas?

Keith

Keith Lofstrom          keithl at keithl.com         Voice (503)-520-1993
KLIC --- Keith Lofstrom Integrated Circuits --- "Your Ideas in Silicon"
Design Contracting in Bipolar and CMOS - Analog, Digital, and Scan ICs

From keithl at kl-ic.com  Thu Apr 19 13:03:23 2007
From: keithl at kl-ic.com (Keith Lofstrom)
Date: Thu, 19 Apr 2007 13:03:23 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] incoming fax receiving services
In-Reply-To: <46278DCF.7000803@gmail.com>
References: <20070419145609.GB30610@gate.kl-ic.com>
	<46278DCF.7000803@gmail.com>
Message-ID: <20070419200323.GA31591@gate.kl-ic.com>

Keith Lofstrom said the following on 04/19/2007 07:56 AM:
>For the last 15 years, I have maintained an incoming fax/modem line.
>I get only about 5 legitimate faxes a year now (almost none are local),
>and about 10 spam faxes a week.  I no longer use the modem for anything.  
>Time to ditch the fax line and save some money.
>
>There are online services that will receive faxes;  some are $10/mo,
>some are free.  They email TIFFs or sometimes a special format.  They
>usually receive on a dedicated number.  Any suggestions for a Linux
>friendly, open format service?

On Thu, Apr 19, 2007 at 08:42:07AM -0700, John Poelstra wrote:
> I've been meaning to post a blog on this topic for some time because I 
> had a hard time finding an answer to this myself.  Thanks for giving me 
> the motivation to finish it :)
> 
> http://poelcat.wordpress.com/2007/04/19/receive-faxes-and-voicemails-for-free-with-linux/

John:

Thanks for the pointer!  I did some research, and came across this:

  http://www.atpm.com/5.06/efax.shtml

In which some of the respondents claim that jconnect is the same company
as eFax.  eFax has become very slimy (channelling junk faxes and charging
the customers page fees on it, selling email addresses to spammers, etc.)
j2 itself is slimy, and anti-FOSS, see:

 http://www.groklaw.net/articlebasic.php?story=2005080914234645

One of the alternatives suggested by one of the commenters on atpm is
this service:

  http://www.maxemail.com/

MaxEmail Lite is $24/year plus $10 signup for up to 500 inbound pages
per month.  Pages can be emailed as PDFs or TIFFs, and can be stored on
their server and retrieved with SSL.  They claim to never sell their
list to spammers, and actively fight it with some measures to detect
likely junk faxes.  That sounds good, I will learn more ...

I also found these comparison lists:

  http://faxing-service-review.toptenreviews.com/
  http://www.faxbeep.com/internet-fax-forum/
  http://www.faxprices.com/

So, I may decide on MaxEmail.  However, it was researching jconnect that
got me going on this, so your experience with that service has led me
indirectly to a service we both may be using soon.  Funny how that works.

Keith

-- 
Keith Lofstrom          keithl at keithl.com         Voice (503)-520-1993
KLIC --- Keith Lofstrom Integrated Circuits --- "Your Ideas in Silicon"
Design Contracting in Bipolar and CMOS - Analog, Digital, and Scan ICs

I've used eFax for a couple of years...it's definitely a little cheesy,
the old Windows eFax client that will run happily under WINE is hard to
find, and the company does spam me.

But...it's free, took almost no time to set up, and
SpamAssassin/Thunderbird seem to handle the spam well enough.

I'd probably use one of the other listed suggestions if not for inertia,
but I figured my tale of eFax might be useful to someone with an
immediate need, not a lot of time, and low standards.  ;0

From: johnxj at comcast.net (John Jason Jordan)
Date: Thu, 19 Apr 2007 14:56:17 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Broken root!
Message-ID: <20070419145617.0aeeaeaf.johnxj@comcast.net>

I installed the final of Feisty amd64 Desktop on my new computer this
morning. Then I used the new computer to back up my Edgy amd64 laptop,
preparatory to doing an apt-get dist-upgrade on the laptop. Everything
was going fine on the new computer until I decided I wanted to install
more software. I clicked on System > Administration > Synaptic Package
Manager, expecting the little window for my root password to pop up.
Instead I got a window that said:

Failed to run /usr/sbin/synaptic as user root.
Unable to copy the user's Xauthorization file.

What happened? Root password was working before because I did the
backup as root with the cp command after mounting the laptop via nfs. I
copied everything to a folder I created on the new computer, ~/backups.
I can't understand how it would be possible, but could the backup have
goobered up the root account?

On Thu, 19 Apr 2007, John Jason Jordan wrote:

> I installed the final of Feisty amd64 Desktop on my new computer 
> this morning. Then I used the new computer to back up my Edgy amd64 
> laptop, preparatory to doing an apt-get dist-upgrade on the laptop. 
> Everything was going fine on the new computer until I decided I 
> wanted to install more software. I clicked on System > 
> Administration > Synaptic Package Manager, expecting the little 
> window for my root password to pop up. Instead I got a window that 
> said:

> Failed to run /usr/sbin/synaptic as user root.

It's likely your hostname change, and your X auth became invalid. 
Log out of your X session, restart X (typically Ctrl+Alt+Bksp), and 
login again.

Paul Heinlein <> heinlein at madboa.com <> http://www.madboa.com/

So next I tried Paul's suggestion. I did Ctrl-Alt-Bkspc and got a login
prompt except that the screen was jiggling and the Ubuntu login
drumbeat was continous. I concluded that this was not a good sign. :) 

I tried Ctrl-Alt-Bkspc again, but got the same results. The login
screen does have an Options menu, and one of its options was Restart.
So I restarted, and when the login screen came up I got:

"GDM could not write to your authorization file. This could mean that
you are out of disk space or that your home directory could not be
opened for writing. In any event, it's not possible to log in. Please
contact your system administrator."

Great. Unfortunately, I guess the system administrator is me.

However, when I read that error message I remembered that the backup
was almost complete when suddenly I got about 20-30 messages that it
could not continue because it was out of disk space. Aha! Except that I
could swear that I later went in and deleted a Linux ISO that I really
didn't need. Except maybe I thought I did and really didn't. Oh well, no
problem, I'll just free up some disk space so I'm sure there is lots of
room.

But how to do that? Well, I started with a Knoppix CD, and it
automounted the partitions. Except it didn't really. It did not see the
RAID 1 partition that Feisty is installed on. It did see the underlying
partitions on each disk. So I thought I would just delete the same
thing -- something big -- off of each disk. But that failed because,
while Knoppix wasn't smart enough to see the RAID 1 partition, it *was*
smart enough not to let me muck around in the individual ext3
partitions, even if I did so as root. So now I'm sitting here trying to
think of a live CD to use that would understand RAID partitions. 

Anyone need a computer mucked up? Call JJJ's professional computer
screwup service. I'll make sure it won't even boot! Guaranteed!

I haven't done anything with the Feisty installation other than make
the backup, so push come to shove I can just start over. But if anyone
has any idea how to delete something off the RAID 1 partition when I
can't log in, please share. :)


From creswick at gmail.com  Thu Apr 19 16:12:30 2007

On 4/19/07, John Jason Jordan <johnxj at comcast.net> wrote:
> while Knoppix wasn't smart enough to see the RAID 1 partition, it *was*
> smart enough not to let me muck around in the individual ext3
> partitions, even if I did so as root. So now I'm sitting here trying to
> think of a live CD to use that would understand RAID partitions.

IIRC, Knoppix mounts existing partitions in read-only mode, so you
just need to remount them read/write.  I quick google search should
turn up examples -- I don't have any handy, and I'm a bit short on
time at the moment :(

This is the crux of why I never plan to use software raid again -- I
prefer external backups for various reasons  (if it's not *in* the
broken computer, you can probably get to it, regardless of what
happened to the broken computer) and Raiding for performance is just a
pain when something breaks.

Hardware raid, on the other hand, works just like a really nice drive,
regardless of what the OS thinks.

The motherboard does have hardware RAID 0 and RAID 1. I started out
using that, but there were problems (which I can't remember). In
further discussion here everyone convinced me that Linux software RAID
was much better than the fakeraid on the motherboard.

As for the problem, I think I'm just going to start over. I need to
delete the Debian Etch partition too. And I want to make the rest of
the disk RAID 1 for the backups. Then I won't run out of room. This
morning I did the backup just to the Feisty partition because I was in
a hurry to get the laptop backed up so I could apt-get dist-upgrade it.

After I reinstall I'm going to experiment by disconnecting one of the
drives to see if the computer will boot with just one. After all, the
purpose of RAID 1 is disaster recovery, and it only make sense to test
the recovery plan to see if it actually works.

Has anyone seen something like this ...

I just installed Suse 10.2. I configured Evolution just like I had with Ubuntu. Mail for a while did not work. I fussed, changed nothing really and then it started to work but I alwasy got this error message.

"RCPT TO <my_comcast_address> failed. Requested action not taken. mailbox unavailable"

I'd get a window popup with that message three time (one popup; the message was repeated in it three times. The mail still worked and was sent.

Now, the popup does not appear. Mail appears to be working. I called comcast twice but of course had to be uising windows and Outlook Express on another machine when I called. Outlook Express worked fine. Comcast will not talk to me if I mention I am using Linux or Evolution of even Outlook. It must be Outlook Express.

But honestly I made no config changes between when it worked and when it didn't. I did reboot a few times. But the working was not linked with one reboot. I don't see why multiple reboots would make mail start working.

So much of what I said here sounds like nonsense (to me too). My question is has anyone seen that error message I quoted above and if so, what does it mean.

Thanks.

From johnxj at comcast.net  Thu Apr 19 21:17:22 2007
From: johnxj at comcast.net (John Jason Jordan)
Date: Thu, 19 Apr 2007 21:17:22 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Feisty tip for Synaptic
Message-ID: <20070419211722.e4d834b7.johnxj@comcast.net>

I just discovered something while playing with my new installation of
Feisty. If you go into System > Administration > Software Sources you
can change the mirror to download from. I set it to
mirrors.cat.pdx.edu, but then I had only about 1500 packages. When I
went back into Software Sources I noticed that you can also specify
http or ftp. It was set to http so I changed it to ftp. After reloading
I had more packages available than before -- 21,115. More important,
using the Ubuntu server for the United States (the default) the
download rate was around 20K/s. Now I get 4-500K/s. 

This tip doesn't work on Edgy because Software Sources only gives you
two choices.

whatever mirror you want and put it there. mirrors.cat.pdx.edu, of
course, is good :)

I can't explain the http/ftp weirdness you saw. Perhaps universe and
multiverse repositories weren't enabled..

From keithl at kl-ic.com  Thu Apr 19 22:23:22 2007
From: keithl at kl-ic.com (Keith Lofstrom)
Date: Thu, 19 Apr 2007 22:23:22 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] incoming fax receiving services
In-Reply-To: <20070419200323.GA31591@gate.kl-ic.com>
References: <20070419145609.GB30610@gate.kl-ic.com>
	<46278DCF.7000803@gmail.com>
	<20070419200323.GA31591@gate.kl-ic.com>
Message-ID: <20070420052322.GA5534@gate.kl-ic.com>

On Thu, Apr 19, 2007 at 01:03:23PM -0700, Keith Lofstrom wrote:
> ...
> MaxEmail Lite is $24/year plus $10 signup for up to 500 inbound pages
> per month.  Pages can be emailed as PDFs or TIFFs, and can be stored on
> their server and retrieved with SSL.  They claim to never sell their
> list to spammers, and actively fight it with some measures to detect
> likely junk faxes.  That sounds good, I will learn more ...

I tried out the MaxEmail demo, and it works fine, so I signed up.
The default process receives faxes at a dedicated phone number in
the Chicago suburbs (local or toll-free numbers available for an
additional fee) and sents them as PDFs to the email addresses
associated with the account (up to five).  I changed the service
to just send notifications, storing the fax messages on their
server (accessable as PDFs with any SSL-secured web browser). 
Someday I will set up procmail so emails from MaxEmail will kick
off a secure wget or something similar.

The MaxEmail web interface is nonintuitive.  A redesign would help,
and thumbnails for the stored faxes would be nice.  But for $2/month,
I can jump through a hoop or two.

BTW, I also learned today that MyFax, another highly recommended (??)
alternative, is owned by Protus, one of the biggest fax spammers in
North America, broadcasting spam into the US from Ontario.  Now that
I am signed up with MaxEmail, I will probably learn something nasty
about MaxEmail, sigh.

Keith

that has a stronger signal and then run a wire to my laptop sitting
somewhere else in the room. Right now it has the antennas it came with but

an antenna with a little better  gain.  9dbi or better would be nice. The
requirements are that it be easy to transport, that it have the correct
RP-TNC connector, and it should be less than $100.  Does anybody have any
suggestions or experience with such antennas?

Ted,

You may have a different version of evolution from the previous distro.
Try deleting the meta data for you local mail box.  Delete the *cmeta,
*ev-summary *ibex* files in the ~/.evolution/mail/local directory.

From russell at personaltelco.net  Fri Apr 20 10:20:21 2007
From: russell at personaltelco.net (Russell Senior)
Date: 20 Apr 2007 10:20:21 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] antenna for wrt54g
In-Reply-To: <af070b010704200710i6274f534l20626cc28acd05e8@mail.gmail.com>
References: <af070b010704200710i6274f534l20626cc28acd05e8@mail.gmail.com>
Message-ID: <868xcn6jze.fsf@coulee.tdb.com>

>>>>> "Bill" == Bill Barry <barryb at proaxis.com> writes:

Bill> I have a wrt54g wireless router in which I have installed
Bill> openwrt in client mode. So for instance in a motel room I can
Bill> put the wrt54g in a position that has a stronger signal and then
Bill> run a wire to my laptop sitting somewhere else in the
Bill> room. Right now it has the antennas it came with but often I am
Bill> still getting pretty weak signals so I am interested in getting
Bill> an antenna with a little better gain.  9dbi or better would be
Bill> nice. The requirements are that it be easy to transport, that it
Bill> have the correct RP-TNC connector, and it should be less than
Bill> $100.  Does anybody have any suggestions or experience with such
Bill> antennas?

Do you want an omni or a directional?  You've got lots of options,
none of them are likely to be perfect.  

I got a cheapo magmount 7 dBi with an RP-TNC connector (circa $15-$20),
but it fell apart pretty quickly in the rain on my car roof.  It would
probably do better inside.  Two of those would give you diversity.
Like this one:

  <http://sharper-concepts.com/product/YSC-RE07U>

You could get a window-mount 9dBi direction for pretty cheap.  One
practical problem with a directional is figuring out which way to
point it.  There is a package (wl, i think) for the WRT radio that
will report RSSI (relative signal strength indicator) and can help you
determine the optimal direction.  These are lightweight, good for
transport.

  <https://shop.invictusnetworks.com/detail.php?id=16075>

Or you could get a nice 13 dBi directional, the practical problem
being a way to point it persistently.  They are flat, but meant to be
mounted on a mast or something.  You'd need to cobble together a way
to mount it.  You'd also need an appropriate pigtail (approximately $15).

  <https://shop.invictusnetworks.com/detail.php?id=3004>


Invictus is located here in town (Lake Oswego, near I5, actually).
Rick Lindahl.  He might have that cheapo magmount too.


-- 
Russell Senior, Secretary
russell at personaltelco.net

Hi all.

but a friend of mine wants me to setup his new computer for him and
wants all his stuff from his old system too exactly as is. My question
is: is Windows (XP) smart enough to not croak and die if it finds itself
running on another computer?


    If not, then what would an EASY way to figure out what stuff is
installed on his current system so I can manually do the honors (when I
say easy, I mean something along the lines of running 'apt-get
--get-selections > someFile' on a Debian-based system)?


    TIA.



From russj at dimstar.net  Sun Apr 22 17:45:34 2007
From: russj at dimstar.net (Russell Johnson)
Date: Sun, 22 Apr 2007 17:45:34 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] OT: Transferring image of Windows system from one computer
	to another
In-Reply-To: <462BFC27.1000806@yahoo.com>
References: <462BFC27.1000806@yahoo.com>
Message-ID: <462C01AE.6080603@dimstar.net>

Robert Jeffrey Miesen wrote:
> My question
> is: is Windows (XP) smart enough to not croak and die if it finds itself
> running on another computer?
>   
Unless it's the same hardware, it'll croak. First thing you'll have to 
do is install all the drivers for any hardware that's different. Video 
card, hard disk controller, ACPI, etc... You'll want to have the drivers 
before you start, or another computer than you can download them on, as 
most likely the network won't work either.
>     If not, then what would an EASY way to figure out what stuff is
> installed on his current system so I can manually do the honors (when I
> say easy, I mean something along the lines of running 'apt-get
> --get-selections > someFile' on a Debian-based system)?
>   
You could give the programs and settings transfer wizard a try. Go to 
Start>Help and search for that.

Russ


From aarghj at yahoo.com  Sun Apr 22 18:13:00 2007
From: aarghj at yahoo.com (john morgali)
Date: Sun, 22 Apr 2007 18:13:00 -0700 (PDT)
Subject: [PLUG] OT: Transferring image of Windows system from one computer
	to another
In-Reply-To: <462BFC27.1000806@yahoo.com>
Message-ID: <187249.87275.qm@web52409.mail.re2.yahoo.com>

There is a way to do this, assuming he has his
original xp cd, or one of the same version and vendor.


1. Image the system over to the new drive, using
whatever method you prefer. 

2. Boot and see if it will redo drivers(you will have
to re-activate windows most likely). 

3. Failing a successful boot, insert the cd, and do a
"repair installation". This is done by going past the
first prompt for opening the recovery console(skip it
as though you were going to do a fresh install).  The
installer should then search for other installations
of windows, find the one you have on the disk, and ask
you if you would like to do an automated repair on it.


This should get it to reinstall the drivers, and get
you running.  You'll still have to reactivate your OS,
but you should have everything that was on the old
drive as it was. 
There are plenty of instructions on the repair install
on this google search:
http://www.google.com/search?client=opera&rls=en&q=windows+xp+repair+install&sourceid=opera&ie=utf-8&oe=utf-8

Barring that method, best thing is to make a list of
installed software, and use the files and settings
xfer wizard to make a backup of the things he wants to
keep.  for whatever reason the file and settings xfer
wizard doesnt grab the outlook or outlook express pst
files, so copy those manually.  Then you should be
good to go.  

Good luck!

John Morgali

From wamorita at hevanet.com  Sun Apr 22 18:45:26 2007
From: wamorita at hevanet.com (William A Morita)
Date: Sun, 22 Apr 2007 18:45:26 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] OT: Transferring image of Windows system from one
	computerto another
In-Reply-To: <462BFC27.1000806@yahoo.com>
References: <462BFC27.1000806@yahoo.com>
Message-ID: <00f101c78549$12b55910$6d01a8c0@RAMA>

Jeff,

My understanding of XP is that it will only tolerate a limited amount of HW
changes.
Putting the disk in to a new system will likely have it complaining.
You might be able to call the MS activation folks for a solution at that
point.

I strongly suggest that you download and run the Belarc Advisor
http://www.belarc.com/free_download.html
This will scan the entire HW and SW for the XP system and give you a listing
of:
	Hardware present
	Software present (including product keys) and MS updates.
I use this to document (hard copy) all of my Windows based systems.

If you are forced into loading a new copy of XP, 
it would be best to apply all the current patches BEFORE connecting to the
internet.
How to do that?
I suggest that you create your own service pack.  Download the DIY Service
pack (assumes SP2)
http://www.heise.de/ct/ftp/projekte/offlineupdate/
Download version 3.04

Instructions in English found at
http://www.heise-security.co.uk/articles/80682
[Note: the download pointer from this page does version 3.02 which has a
problem.]

You should then be able to burn the .iso file(s) CD or DVD with all the MS
patches.
The CD/DVD should autostart into a process that will apply the patches.
This process will go through several reboots.

Then you should have a fully patch XP prior to plugging into the internet.
[Note: this process also works for W2K which will migrate to a new system
with some fiddling]

Hope this helps

- Bill Morita 
wamorita at hevanet.com

On a system with IDE drives, switching back to the base microsoft IDE
drivers will be the
first thing to do.  Then doing a copy from one drive to another with
something like
bootitNG.

The other option is to use the transfer wizard to move programs and
files between
machines. This only works if you have enough space on the system.

> of:
> 	Hardware present
> 	Software present (including product keys) and MS updates.
> I use this to document (hard copy) all of my Windows based systems.

I just wanted to say "me too" to William's fine advice; Belarc looks
like a nifty tool that I hope I won't have to use, but probably will.

As for Winders in general, I'd STRONGLY recommend you plan on doing a
fresh install on the new box and copy the data over.  Just as the gods
need blood sacrifices every once in a while, Windows needs a good
wipe-and-reinstall every 6-18 months.

Allocate 5-8 hours for the OS install, OS patching, SW install and SW
patching.  Then another 2 hours to get the data and the settings in the
right places.  Finally, save another hour for handholding and
getting-acquainted, because things will NOT be the same as before, and
that will be frightening for the user.

If that sounds like as much work as a Linux conversion, it is, sorta,
except that Ubuntu 7.04 will do it all in less than half the time.

And I suspect Wine runs Windows apps better than Vista does,
  Aaron

From pm at nellump.net  Mon Apr 23 12:20:13 2007
From: pm at nellump.net (Paul Mullen)
Date: Mon, 23 Apr 2007 12:20:13 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] USB IDE Enclosures - Recommended Reading
In-Reply-To: <462CF927.4050302@comcast.net>
References: <462CF927.4050302@comcast.net>
Message-ID: <20070423192013.GA15646@nellump.net>

On Mon, Apr 23, 2007 at 11:21:27AM -0700, Richard C. Steffens wrote:
> Can someone point me to recommended reading on USB IDE enclosures? I
> found the page (http://www.enuinc.com/enclx.html) on ENU's site
> listing several and I see that I need to read a bit more to
> understand all the options.

I don't know of any recommended reading. There are only a few points
you really need to worry about:

1) What interfaces the enclosure supports (both to the hard drive and
to the host system). More interfaces, more money.

2) What capacity of hard drive the enclosure's chip set supports.
ENU's web site states this in the details for each enclosure.

3) Quality, reliability, etc. are all important, but a little more
difficult to determine short of gathering up personal recommendations.

As for personal recommendations, I bought this CoolMax-branded
IDE/USB 2.0 enclosure from ENU several months ago:
http://www.newegg.com/Product/Product.aspx?Item=N82E16817146066

I'm pretty sure it's what ENU calls an "OEM" enclosure:
http://www.enuinc.com/encl-oem-002-black.html

It has a steel case with plastic end caps, made to vaguely resemble a
book; includes a plastic stand that the enclosure slides into; odd
graphical decorations on the sides. Feels pretty sturdy, and works
just fine.


-- 
Paul

From robert.citek at gmail.com  Mon Apr 23 14:39:34 2007
From: robert.citek at gmail.com (Robert Citek)
Date: Mon, 23 Apr 2007 16:39:34 -0500
Subject: [PLUG] USB IDE Enclosures - Recommended Reading
In-Reply-To: <462CF927.4050302@comcast.net>
References: <462CF927.4050302@comcast.net>
Message-ID: <462D2796.5070001@gmail.com>

Richard C. Steffens wrote:
> Can someone point me to recommended reading on USB IDE enclosures? I
> found the page (http://www.enuinc.com/enclx.html) on ENU's site listing
> several and I see that I need to read a bit more to understand all the
> options.

Like Paul, I don't know of any reading.  But another thing to consider
is heat dissipation.  I once got an enclosure that worked great as long
as you left the drive exposed.  If I assembled the entire enclosure, the
drive would overheat and then fail.

For a bit of an out-of-the-box idea, if you don't need an enclosure, you
could use a USB drive adapter.  This one takes SATA, 3.5" PATA, and
laptop drives and converts to USB 2.0:

http://www.amazon.com/gp/product/B000HJ99DI

We've got them at work and they've been great for backups, restores,
transferring large files, etc.  The largest drive we've tried has been
250 GB and it worked just fine.

Too bad they don't come in FireWire or dual USB2/FW.

Regards,
- Robert

From eli.stair at gmail.com  Mon Apr 23 20:10:36 2007

Message-ID: <f4468cae0704232010o1d9afd5dsb0ecd07b6aa10ff2@mail.gmail.com>

You've got a few significant dependencies (likely updated with your
distro) that could be the cause.  Your MySQL, PHP, rrdtool, and apache
installs at first glance may have been altered.

My suggestion to buffer your services from distro changes is to build
and use packages from source for anything you need un-altered.  That
can become unwieldy, and may not be the right choice for ya though.

/eli

From mikeraz at patch.com  Mon Apr 23 20:53:26 2007
From: mikeraz at patch.com (Michael Rasmussen)
Date: Mon, 23 Apr 2007 20:53:26 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] When procmail is led astray - resolved
In-Reply-To: <462D74F5.5080008@schluting.com>
References: <30832.170.135.112.12.1177360365.squirrel@mail.patch.com>
	<462D74F5.5080008@schluting.com>
Message-ID: <20070424035326.GA8646@patch.com>

Charlie Schluting wrote:
> > So what would/did procmail do with mail when the .procmailrc was unreadable?  Bit
> > bucket or some default safe place.  (har!)

As it turns out, now more than ever, comments are your friend!
   DEFAULT=$MAILDIR/read   #completely optional
Since the ^M at line end was commented out all of the mail that with foobared
rules ended up in my $MAILDIR/read - I didn't lose a thing.   
I got lucky twice, once with actually defining the default, and then with
having a comment on the same line that filtered the end of line corruption.

If you set up a system for someone who also uses Windows or MAC you might
consider inserting a DEFAULT definition with a comment just in case.


> It may exit with a temp_fail. It may deliver to a strange mailbox (not
> likely). Mail logs should say - and you may want to enable logging in
> your .procmailrc too.

Ah! I did, though it wasn't helpful.

> Just vi the file and delete all the ^M's :)

Well, I needed to figure that out to get your message.

I actually used dos2unix to recover from the mangling editor (have a Sparc on
my desk too) and sent that across.

Further moral:  don't trust wsftppro (pro? HA!) to do the right thing with
"auto" detecting ASCII vs Binary files.

This afternoon was the big day. I used Update Manager on my Edgy amd64
laptop to upgrade it to Feisty. All was well until it hung at
"installing new version of config file: /etc/apt/apt.conf.d01ubuntu..."
The mouse and keyboard were dead. Eventually I had to hit the power
button. Of course, it wouldn't reboot. It hangs just before it scans
the hard disk -- way before it gets to X. if I leave it sit for a few
minutes it pops up a BusyBox message:

/bin/sh: cant access tray; job control turned off
(initramfs)

Typing help gives me a list of commands that I can run from the
initramfs prompt, but none of them looked very useful. 

I found someone else with a similar problem on Ubuntu forums.
Suggestions included using a live CD, then mounting the filesystem for
the broken installation, then chrooting to it, then trying commands
like 

apt-get install ubuntu-desktop
apt-get dist-upgrade
apt-get -f install
dpkg --configure -a

Dpkg --reconfigure -a ran for a bit and did a bunch of things, but the
system still won't boot. I know the new kernel is 2.6.20-15-generic,
and 2.6.17-11-generic is what was listed in Grub's old menu.lst, so I
changed it to the new one, but that was even worse -- Grub wouldn't
even start -- file not found. Recovery mode also fails, as does the
earlier kernel listed in menu.lst.

I do have a full backup of ~/ and most of the rest of the installation
made last night. But reformatting and reinstalling will take 8-12 hours
just to get all the apps installed and configured. I'm pretty sure I
can get the system fixed if I can just get it to boot. Whatever fixes
have to be command line, because I can't launch any GUI apps while
chrooted to the filesystem -- the live CD is using the video.

Does anyone have any additional suggestions?

I still recommend Cacti. I've set it up in environments with 100+ 
switches, and countless servers. It does have some scaling issues 
depending on how quickly the hosts you monitor respond to the SNMP 
query. That is mostly solveable by using CactiD, the dedicated poller 
for Cacti that is much faster and efficient than the PHP poller.

I am also setting up a Zabbix monitoring station to see how well it 
scales and reports. It seems promising, but with only a few hosts 
monitored by it so far, I will reserve judgement.

As for the fragility of Cacti. It really depends on what you've updated. 
If you want to provide more details about your round of updates, which 
versions you changed to, etc.
I know for a fact that if you are running an older version of Cacti, and 
then update RRDTool, Cacti will complain. But you can change the version 
of RRTool that Cacti expects in the configuration.

Brent Jones
brent [at] servuhome [dot] net

From qhartman at gmail.com  Tue Apr 24 06:58:56 2007
From: qhartman at gmail.com (Quentin Hartman)
Date: Tue, 24 Apr 2007 06:58:56 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Borked Edgy > Feisty Upgrade
In-Reply-To: <20070423221814.7e93a7bf@Devil6.hsd1.or.comcast.net>
References: <20070423221814.7e93a7bf@Devil6.hsd1.or.comcast.net>
Message-ID: <8975a95f0704240658x79090813n90eac25b75e0d5c4@mail.gmail.com>

I had a broken feisty upgrde due to my computer hanging in the middle of it
(I have a hardware gremlin somewhere on this box) which I was able to fix
via the bootcd / chroot trick. One thing to be aware of is that you need to
bind mount /dev /and /proc from the running system inside your chroot or
else a large number of package configurations will fail. Once I was in there
I ran "dpkg --configure -a" a few times in a row until it stopped looking
like it was working, and then I did an "aptitude upgrade" followed by an
"aptitude dist-upgrade". As I recall there was also a specific package I had
to explicitly either remove/install/ or configure because it was blocking
the dep finding algorithm in aptitude. Anyway,  I've found that process to
be a bit more stable than jumping right into the -dist. "upgrade" seems to
be less agreessive with what it updates, making the process shorter, and
hitting only core components. Once that is happy I move on with the dist- to
get everything else.

Hope this helps!

-- 
-Regards-

-Quentin Hartman-

In-Reply-To: <20070423192013.GA15646@nellump.net>

Paul Mullen wrote:
> On Mon, Apr 23, 2007 at 11:21:27AM -0700, Richard C. Steffens wrote:
>> Can someone point me to recommended reading on USB IDE enclosures?

> I don't know of any recommended reading. There are only a few points
> you really need to worry about:
> 
> 1) What interfaces the enclosure supports (both to the hard drive and
> to the host system). More interfaces, more money.
> 
> 2) What capacity of hard drive the enclosure's chip set supports.
> ENU's web site states this in the details for each enclosure.
> 
> 3) Quality, reliability, etc. are all important, but a little more
> difficult to determine short of gathering up personal recommendations.
> 
> As for personal recommendations, I bought this CoolMax-branded
> IDE/USB 2.0 enclosure from ENU several months ago:
> http://www.newegg.com/Product/Product.aspx?Item=N82E16817146066
> 
> I'm pretty sure it's what ENU calls an "OEM" enclosure:
> http://www.enuinc.com/encl-oem-002-black.html
> 
> It has a steel case with plastic end caps, made to vaguely resemble a
> book; includes a plastic stand that the enclosure slides into; odd
> graphical decorations on the sides. Feels pretty sturdy, and works
> just fine.

Robert Citek wrote:
> Like Paul, I don't know of any reading.  But another thing to consider
> is heat dissipation.  I once got an enclosure that worked great as
> long as you left the drive exposed.  If I assembled the entire
> enclosure, the drive would overheat and then fail.
>
> For a bit of an out-of-the-box idea, if you don't need an enclosure,
> you could use a USB drive adapter.  This one takes SATA, 3.5" PATA,
> and laptop drives and converts to USB 2.0:
>
> http://www.amazon.com/gp/product/B000HJ99DI
>
> We've got them at work and they've been great for backups, restores,
> transferring large files, etc.  The largest drive we've tried has been
> 250 GB and it worked just fine.
>
> Too bad they don't come in FireWire or dual USB2/FW.

On Tue, 24 Apr 2007 06:58:56 -0700
"Quentin Hartman" <qhartman at gmail.com> wrote:

> I had a broken feisty upgrde due to my computer hanging in the middle
> of it (I have a hardware gremlin somewhere on this box) which I was
> able to fix via the bootcd / chroot trick. One thing to be aware of
> is that you need to bind mount /dev /and /proc from the running
> system inside your chroot or else a large number of package
> configurations will fail. Once I was in there I ran "dpkg --configure
> -a" a few times in a row until it stopped looking like it was
> working, and then I did an "aptitude upgrade" followed by an
> "aptitude dist-upgrade". As I recall there was also a specific
> package I had to explicitly either remove/install/ or configure
> because it was blocking the dep finding algorithm in aptitude.
> Anyway,  I've found that process to be a bit more stable than jumping
> right into the -dist. "upgrade" seems to be less agreessive with what
> it updates, making the process shorter, and hitting only core
> components. Once that is happy I move on with the dist- to get
> everything else.

That makes a lot of sense. I note that a lot of the errors when I run
the upgrade commands while chrooted to hda2 involve trying to display
things on the GUI, but hda2's GUI is not the one that is running.

However, I am unfamiliar with how to "bind mount /dev /and /proc from
the running system inside your chroot." Reading man mount I find

mount --bind olddir newdir

But I am not sure how to use that command in this case. I think I need
to get the hda2 installation that I am chrooted into to use the /dev
and /proc from the live CD, right? So it would be

sudo mount --bind /media/hda2/dev /dev
sudo mount --bind /media/hda2/proc /proc

Right? But then what happens to the contents of /dev and /proc on the
live CD?

Message-ID: <8975a95f0704241053i4c952e64o32f39f7a4b88d704@mail.gmail.com>

On 4/24/07, John Jason Jordan <johnxj at comcast.net> wrote:
>
> However, I am unfamiliar with how to "bind mount /dev /and /proc from
> the running system inside your chroot." Reading man mount I find
>
> mount --bind olddir newdir
>
> But I am not sure how to use that command in this case. I think I need
> to get the hda2 installation that I am chrooted into to use the /dev
> and /proc from the live CD, right? So it would be
>
> sudo mount --bind /media/hda2/dev /dev
> sudo mount --bind /media/hda2/proc /proc
>
> Right? But then what happens to the contents of /dev and /proc on the
> live CD?


Sorry I wasn't clear. I forget sometimes that not everyone has lived with
Gentoo for extended periods. As an aside, doing that (living with Gentoo) is
awesome for buffing up your troubleshooting and recovery skills, you have to
muck with a lot of internals that usually are hidden. But I digress...

The syntax I use is:

sudo mount -o bind /dev /media/hda2/dev
sudo mount -o bind /proc /media/hda2/proc

This must be done _before_ chrooting into the installation on your hard
disk. As far as /dev and /proc on the liveCD, they just keep chugging along.
The bind mount just makes them appear in another place, and they provide
enough information to the chrooted installation for it to complete it's
tasks. Some of what will appear there will not be 100% accurate, but it's
accurate enough for our purposes.

Hope this helps you get going!

-- 
-Regards-

-Quentin Hartman-

From ed at alcpress.com  Tue Apr 24 12:23:02 2007
From: ed at alcpress.com (Ed Sawicki)
Date: Tue, 24 Apr 2007 12:23:02 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] digital signature checking
Message-ID: <462E5916.5070606@alcpress.com>

In the Linux world, we have md5sum, sha1sum, and
PGP/GPG that allow us to verify the authenticity
of downloaded software. These programs are supplied
with Linux distributions so users don't have to
deal with the chicken-and-egg problem of downloading
them and checking them for authenticity.

Similar programs are not shipped with most versions
of Windows, thus creating the chicken-and-egg problem
of downloading them. Has Vista changed this?
Does Vista ship with an authenticity checker?

Ed Sawicki
